No single Hebrew/Greek term for "non-confession" appears in the CHAR-3 corpus.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The structural opposite appears within the corpus itself: Ecc 8:12 — "those who fear God, because they fear before him" — the fear-of-God phrase names the positive opposite of the conscience-suppressed state. The vocabulary of the fear of God (*yare* — to fear, revere) is the positive structural opposite: where the sinner lacks fear of God, the righteous person possesses it. Additionally, the confessional form (*chatati* — "I have sinned") in CHAR-3 is the vocabulary-level structural opposite of *lo chatati* ("I have not sinned") in CHAR-4. The structural opposite is thus named both dispositionally (fear of God) and verbally (confessional acknowledgment).

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The structural opposite is named within the corpus. Sur (H5493) in its positive form — "turned aside from," "departed from" — is the vocabulary of repentance as it appears in the corpus's own negations: "he did not turn aside from" implies that "he turned aside from" would be the positive. Neh 9:29's "in order to turn them back" (*shuv* — H7725: to return, turn back) names the positive structural opposite directly: the shuv (turning-back/returning to God) is what the stubborn shoulder and stiff neck refuse. Shuv is the standard OT repentance-term: the structural opposite of CHAR-5 is shuv.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Yes. 1Jo 3:6 — "no one who *abides* in him keeps on sinning" — *menōn* (abiding, remaining) is the structural opposite: the person who abides in Christ does not keep on sinning. The abiding relationship is the structural opposite of habitual defection. Ecc 7:26 — "he who *pleases God* escapes her" — the God-pleaser (*tov lifney ha-Elohim* — good/pleasing before God) is the person-type opposite of the sinner (hachotet). Pro 13:22 — the *good man* (ish tov) who leaves inheritance to grandchildren is the opposite of the sinner whose wealth is redirected.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The structural opposite appears implicitly through the preventive commands (Exo 23:33; Deu 20:18): the opposite of causing others to sin through authority is the leader whose authority prevents others from sinning — the Hiphil causative deployed in the direction of righteousness rather than sin. 2Ki 23:15 — Josiah's demolition — is the closest evidence of the opposite: a leader whose inner commitment to God drives the dismantling of the sinning structure, implicitly "causing Israel not to sin" by removing the sinning transmission mechanism. No dedicated Hebrew term for the structural opposite appears in the corpus.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The 9-verse corpus contains no dedicated term for the structural opposite of political revolt (submission, loyalty, or allegiance). The structural opposite is implied by the revolt's breaking-from language ("revolted *from* the rule of" — 2Ki 8:20) but not lexically named within the corpus. The positive term for political submission/allegiance is outside the corpus's vocabulary.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(S)*

**[CHAR-9]** S — The corpus contains no dedicated Greek term for the structural opposite of sinful speech within the 22 verses. The structural opposite (edifying, true, God-honouring speech) is implied by the renunciation commands (Eph 4:31; Col 3:8) and the positive alternative implied in Rev 2:9's true identity versus false identity — but no term is named within the corpus.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — The 5-verse corpus contains no dedicated terms for the structural opposite of any of the three mechanisms. The opposites (desire well-governed, authentic identity, covenantal faithfulness) are implied by the mechanisms' negations but not lexically named within these verses.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Yes — the structural opposite appears consistently across the corpus. (1) *Dikaiosynē* (righteousness): Rom 6:18/22 — "slaves of righteousness"; Rom 3:21/22 (broader context) — "the righteousness of God" — righteousness is the structural opposite of the sin-condition, named repeatedly in the liberation passages as what the person moves toward when freed from sin; (2) *Heb 4:15 — "without sin"* — the sinlessness of Christ names the structural opposite as the condition of being-without-sin; (3) *2Cor 5:21 — "become the righteousness of God"* — the transformation from sin-condition to righteousness-condition names the opposite directly.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The structural opposite is named through the new-birth language. 1Jo 3:9 — "born of God" (*gegenēmenos ek tou theou*) — the divine-birth state is the structural opposite of the slave-to-sin state; 1Jo 5:18 — "he who was born of God protects him" (*ho gennētheis ek tou theou tērei auton*) — the born-of-God protective state is the structural opposite of the evil one's touch. The vocabulary of birth-from-God is the primary opposite-term field in CHAR-12's corpus.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The structural opposite is named through the erasure-vocabulary rather than through a distinct term for the record-free state. The primary erasure terms: (1) *Kāsāh* (to cover — Psa 32:1; 85:2 — "covered all their sin"); (2) *Māḥāh* (to blot out — Isa 43:25; 44:22; Eze 33:16 — "blotted out"); (3) *Zākar lōʾ* (to remember no more — Jer 31:34; Isa 43:25 — "I will not remember"); (4) *Šālaḵ ʾaḥărê gēwkā* (to cast behind the back — Isa 38:17); (5) *Šālaḵ bimṣūlôt* (to cast into the depths — Mic 7:19). The structural opposite is the record-cleared state described through five distinct erasure metaphors.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — Mar 3:29 — "never has forgiveness, but is guilty of an eternal sin" (*aiōniou hamartēmatos* — eternal sin). The "eternal sin" (*aiōnion hamartēma*) is the structural opposite of forgiveness received: the permanent guilt with no exit. The forgiveness-vocabulary (kip.pu.rim — covering; aphesis — release) is opposed by the eternal-guilt vocabulary (aiōnion hamartēma — permanent unforgiven sin-act). The structural opposite is thus lexically named rather than merely implied.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Implicitly. Isa 64:5 — "you meet him who joyfully works righteousness, those who remember you in your ways" — the structural opposite is described through a participial phrase rather than a single term: "joyfully works righteousness" (*pōʿēl ṣedeq*) and "remembers you in your ways" — these name the opposite condition through action-orientation rather than through a noun. Eze 37:23 — "they shall be my people, and I will be their God" — the restored covenant-identity formula names the positive structural opposite at the relational level.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — *dikaios* (G1342 — righteous/just, NT) and *tsaddiq* (H6662 — righteous, OT). Mat 9:13 — "I came not to call the righteous (*dikaious*), but sinners (*hamartōlous*)" — the righteous is the explicit structural opposite named in the same verse. Psa 1:5 — "sinners in the congregation of the righteous" — the righteous (*tsaddiqim*) is the explicit opposite within whose congregation sinners cannot stand. Pro 13:21 — "sinners... the righteous are rewarded with good" — the righteous is the contrasting identity-type. The structural opposite is lexically named throughout the corpus.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — implicitly. A.sham (H0816) in its negative form (*lōʾ yʾăšam* — "will not be guilty" or "not guilty") names the structural opposite. Psa 34:22 — "none of those who take refuge in him will be condemned" (a.sham negative) — the not-guilty state is named through the negated guilt-term. Isa 6:7 — "your guilt is taken away" — the taken-away state is the experiential opposite of the guilt-bearing state. Jer 33:8 — "cleanse them from all the guilt... and forgive" — the cleansed-and-forgiven state names the structural opposite.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The structural opposite is named through negation and forgiveness vocabulary. Jer 50:20 — "iniquity shall be sought in Israel, and there shall be none" — the structural opposite is the state of no-iniquity-present; Psa 32:2 — "the man against whom the Lord counts no iniquity" — the not-counted state; Psa 18:23/2Sa 22:24 — "I kept myself from my guilt (*ʿāwōn*)" — the disciplined avoidance of iniquity. The structural opposite is thus named through negative constructions of the same term rather than through a distinct antonym.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The structural opposite is named through obedience and faithfulness vocabulary. Exo 23:21 — "pay careful attention to him and obey his voice; do not rebel against him" — *šāmaʿ* (obey/listen) is the structural opposite of pa.sha (rebel). Hos 14:9 — "the upright walk in them, but transgressors stumble in them" — the upright walker (*yĕšārîm* — straight, upright) is the named opposite person-type. Rom 4:15 — the existence of law implies the existence of obedience as the alternative to transgression; Rom 5:19 — "by the one man's obedience the many will be made righteous" — obedience (the opposite of Adam's trespass) is explicitly named as the structural opposite.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The structural opposite is *faithfulness* (*ʾĕmûnāh* — steadfast faithfulness; *ḥesed* — steadfast covenant-love). Mal 2:15 — "guard yourselves in your spirit, and let none of you be faithless" — the guarded spirit is the faithful spirit, the structural opposite named by negation. Pro 11:3 — "the integrity of the upright guides them, but the crookedness of the treacherous destroys them" — *tummāh* (integrity, blamelessness) is the structural opposite. The cross-register flags (M13 — Truth/Faithfulness) confirm: the positive M13 faithfulness-characteristic is the structural opposite of CHAR-20's faithlessness.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The structural opposite is implied through the wisdom and righteousness vocabulary. Pro 8:13 — wisdom's hatred of perverted speech implies wisdom (*ḥokmāh*) as the structural opposite; Pro 10:31 — "the mouth of the righteous brings forth wisdom, but the perverse tongue will be cut off" — the righteous mouth as the direct opposite; Eph 4:24 — "the new self, created after the likeness of God in true righteousness and holiness" — the new-self righteousness and holiness as the structural opposite of the old self's corruption (Eph 4:22).

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — and uniquely explicitly. Deu 32:4 — "without iniquity (*av.lah*), just and upright is he" — the structural opposite is "just and upright" (*tsaddiq* and *yashar*) explicitly named in opposition; Zep 3:5 — "the Lord is righteous; he does no injustice (*av.lah*)" — *tsaddiq* (righteous) as the structural opposite; Mat 5:45 — "on the just and on the unjust" — *dikaios* (just/righteous) is the direct Greek structural opposite of *adikos*. The M26 cross-register cluster provides the full structural opposite vocabulary.

---

### T7.1.6

**Question:** Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Yes. Chat.ta (H2400) is the person-type term — "sinner" — naming the one who habitually possesses or embodies the sinning characteristic. However, this term belongs primarily to CHAR-16 (Sinner as moral character) rather than to CHAR-1 directly. Within the CHAR-1 corpus, person-type language is present but secondary: "sinful nation" (Isa 1:4 — cha.ta.im, plural of chat.ta) names the people-type constituted by sinning. The person-type term confirms the connection between the act (CHAR-1) and the character (CHAR-16): repeated wilful sinning constitutes the sinner as a moral type.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(S)*

**[CHAR-2]** S — No person-type term appears in the 18-verse corpus for the characteristic of unintentional sinning. There is no Hebrew or Greek term for "the unintentional sinner" as a constituted moral type — because unintentional sinning does not constitute a person's moral character in the way that CHAR-16 (sinner as moral character) does. The inadvertent sinner is a normal person who sinned without knowing; they are not a constituted type. The absence of a person-type term for CHAR-2 is analytically significant: the characteristic does not generate a moral identity but describes a moral episode.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(S)*

**[CHAR-3]** S — No person-type term for "the habitual confessor" appears in the 34-verse corpus. Confession is presented as an episodic moral event rather than as a constituted character-type. The absence is consistent with confession's responsive nature: it is what the person does in response to sin, not what they are.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Yes — more prominently than in any other characteristic in the corpus so far. Ecc 2:26 — *lechotet* ("to the sinner"); Ecc 8:12 — *hachotet* ("the sinner") — the person-type term for the one who exists in the conscience-suppressed orientation is the *sinner* (chat.ta / hachotet). This is the same root as CHAR-16 (Sinner as moral character): the sinner-type term in Ecclesiastes defines a person by their fundamental orientation (not-God-pleasing, not-fear-of-God) rather than by a specific act. The person-type is the constituted character of the conscience-absent person.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Limited person-type evidence. 1Ti 5:20 — "those who persist in sin" (*hamartanontōn* — present participle used substantively) names the person-type as "the ones sinning [continuously]." This is not a dedicated person-type term but the participial construction used to name the type. The Kings corpus does not produce a dedicated person-type term for "the one who refuses to repent" — the characteristic is always expressed through the predicate (did not depart from) rather than as a substantive type-name.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Yes — more prominently than in any other characteristic in the M10 corpus. The participial *hachotet* ("the sinner" — Ecc 7:26; 9:2/18; Pro 11:31/13:22) appears across 5 verses, making CHAR-6 the characteristic most thoroughly expressed through person-type vocabulary. This reflects the definition's emphasis: habitual defection is the will's *settled direction* — a formed character, a constituted type — rather than an episodic act. The person-type vocabulary is the natural form for this because the characteristic is constitutively about what the person *is* rather than what they *do* at a given moment.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The primary person-type is named by the role rather than a dedicated moral-type term: the *leader who made Israel to sin* — the Jeroboam-pattern leader — is the person-type of CHAR-7. Jeroboam's name itself functions as the person-type within the Kings corpus: "the sins of Jeroboam... who made Israel to sin" — Jeroboam is the eponymous contagious-sin leader, the canonical instance of the type. No dedicated Hebrew term equivalent to *hachotet* (the sinner — CHAR-6/16) exists for the contagious-sin leader.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The corpus contains no dedicated person-type term for the habitual practitioner of political revolt. The revolting parties are named by their political-ethnic identity (Moab, Edom, Libnah, Israel) rather than by a moral-type term. The characteristic does not produce a person-type vocabulary in this corpus.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Yes. 1Ti 1:13 — "I was a *blasphemer*" (blasfēmos used as a substantive person-type noun) — the adjective functioning as a person-type term naming the one whose character is defined by sinful speech. 2Ti 3:2 — "abusive" (blasfēmos) — the adjective as a person-type marker in the end-times character list. Both instances use blasfēmos to name the constituted person-type, confirming that sinful speech can form a settled character identity.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — The corpus contains no dedicated person-type terms for the habitual practitioner of these mechanisms. The enticers of 2Pe 2:14/18 are described adjectivally and verbally ("hearts trained in greed... they entice") rather than through a nominal person-type term. Apostasia (G0646) is a noun but names the condition/practice rather than the person-type.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Yes. *Hamartōlos* (G0268 — sinner) appears in the broader Gospel context (Luk 7:37/39 — the sinful woman; Luk 18:13 — "God, be merciful to me, a sinner" — outside the 112-verse corpus but structurally implied as the person-type named by the universal condition). Within the 112-verse corpus: Gal 2:17 — "found to be sinners" — the person-type of the sinner is invoked as the charge that might be brought. The sinner-person-type (CHAR-16) is the person-level expression of CHAR-11's universal condition.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Yes, implicitly. Joh 8:34 — "everyone who practices sin" (*pas ho poiōn tēn hamartian*) — the present-participle construction names a person-type: "the one who makes sin" (i.e. the habitual sinner). 1Jo 3:8 — "whoever makes a practice of sinning" (*ho poiōn tēn hamartian*) — the same construction names the person-type whose identity marker is the enslaving practice. These constructions function as person-type identifiers: the type is constituted by the ongoing practice.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Yes — through the bearer-of-sin language. Num 9:13 — "that man shall bear his sin" (*nāśāʾ ḥeṭʾô* — "shall carry his chet"); Deu 24:16 — "each one shall be put to death for his own sin" (*ḥeṭʾô*). These constructions identify the person-type as "the one who bears sin" — the individual moral subject who carries a personal record. Isa 53:12 — "he bore the sin of many" — the servant's person-type is the sin-bearer, creating a positive person-type in contrast to the negative.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Implicitly. Luk 7:47 — "he who is forgiven little" (*hō oligon aphietai*) and "she loved much" — the contrast names two person-types: the much-forgiven and the little-forgiven. These are person-types defined by the magnitude of their forgiveness-reception. The priest (Lev 10:17 — "given to you that you may bear the iniquity") is the person-type of the forgiveness-mediator — the one through whom others seek and receive.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes. Isa 65:20 — "the sinner (*hachôṭeʾ*) a hundred years old shall be accursed" — the participial form *hachôṭeʾ* (the sinning-one, the sinner) is the person-type term: it names the person whose characteristic is the ongoing sinning-pattern that results in curse. The participle form (present active — "the one who is sinning") names the person-type as constituted by ongoing sinning rather than a single act. Within the generational context, this sinner-person-type is the individual who perpetuates the inherited sinning-pattern to the end of their days.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Chat.ta (H2400) and hamartōlos (G0268) are themselves the person-type terms: they are agent-nouns naming the person whose character is constituted by sinning. The *qattāl* pattern of chat.ta is the Hebrew grammatical form specifically for intensive/habitual agents. 1Ti 1:15 — "of whom I am the foremost" (*prōtos* — first/foremost) names Paul as the paradigmatic worst-case person-type within the sinner category. Isa 65:20 — "the sinner (*hachôṭeʾ*) a hundred years old shall be accursed" — the participial form names the person-type by their ongoing sinning character.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — A.shem (H0818) — Gen 42:21 — "we are guilty" — functions as the person-type description (though not a dedicated agent-noun like chat.ta). The participial form of a.sham used in Judg 21:22 (*ʾāšēm*) and Hab 1:11 (*ʾāšēm* — "guilty men") approaches a person-type term: it names the person constituted by the guilt-state. Amo 8:14 — "the Guilt of Samaria" (*ʾašmat Šōmĕrôn*) uses ash.mah as a personalised title for the idol — an ironic inversion where the guilt-condition name becomes an idol-name.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — No dedicated person-type term within the a.von H5771G corpus; the characteristic is content-focused (the crime-reality) rather than person-type-focused (compare CHAR-16's chat.ta/hamartōlos agent-noun). The nearest person-type usage: Isa 1:4 — "a people laden with iniquity (*ʿăwōn*)" — describing the person/community by their iniquity-burden. The person-type dimension is expressed through the adjective or participial: Eze 33:8 — "you shall surely die" addressed to *rāšāʿ* (the wicked) who "shall die in his iniquity" — the wicked person is named separately from the iniquity they carry.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes — multiple person-type terms. (1) *Pa.sha (H6586) as agent*: Isa 46:8 — "you transgressors" — direct person-type address; Isa 53:12 — "numbered with the transgressors"; (2) *Parabatēs (G3848)*: "transgressor" as agent-noun — Rom 2:25/27; Gal 2:18; Jam 2:9/11; (3) *Sa.rah (H5627)*: Deu 13:5 — "that prophet... taught rebellion" — the revolt-teacher as person-type; (4) *Psa 51:13* — "transgressors" as a class the forgiven person will teach — the person-type as a reachable group. CHAR-19 is the richest person-type vocabulary in M10.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — ba.gad generates the person-type. Isa 24:16 — "the traitors (*bōgĕdîm*) have betrayed" — the plural agent-noun names the person-class of traitors; Psa 119:158 — "I look at the faithless (*bōgĕdîm*) with disgust" — the faithless as a recognisable class; Pro 2:22 — "the treacherous (*bōgĕdîm*) will be rooted out of it." The *bōgĕd* (participle/agent-noun form of ba.gad) is CHAR-20's person-type term — the one whose inner character is constituted by treachery.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — multiple person-type terms. Tah.pu.khah produces the person-type "the man of perverted speech" (Pro 2:12 — *ʾîš tahpukôt*); "the perverse tongue" (Pro 10:31/32) names the person-type by their organ; 1Ti 6:5 — "people who are depraved in mind" names the person-type by their faculty; 2Pe 2:12 — "like irrational animals, creatures of instinct, born to be caught and destroyed" — the ultimate perverted person-type named as the animal-instinctual creature.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — two distinct person-type terms. (1) *Av.val (H5767)*: OT agent-noun — "the unrighteous one" (Job 27:7; 29:17; 31:3; Zep 3:5) — the OT person-type characterised by injustice as a defining moral identity; (2) *Adikos (G0094)*: NT adjective-as-noun — "the unjust" (Mat 5:45; Luk 18:11; Act 24:15; Rom 3:5; 1Cor 6:1/9; Heb 6:10; 1Pe 3:18; 2Pe 2:9) — the NT person-type; used in eight NT verses, making it the most frequently deployed person-type term across the Phase 9 characteristics.

---

### T7.1.7

**Question:** Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus does not contain a dedicated seeking-term for wilful sinning itself (sinning is committed, not sought). However, the corpus evidences the seeking of the *remedy* for sinning through supplication: 1Ki 8:33/35/50 (Solomon's prayer as the seeking of forgiveness for sinning — the prayer is the supplication term for the forgiven state). Psa 41:4 ("O Lord, be gracious to me; heal me, for I have sinned against you") uses the supplication form (chen + raph.ah) in the aftermath of sinning. The supplication operates at the post-sinning stage, not the sinning stage itself.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The offering-and-atonement language functions as the supplication register in CHAR-2. Num 15:27 ("he shall offer a female goat a year old for a sin offering") — the bringing of the offering is the formal act of seeking atonement. Num 15:28 ("the priest shall make atonement before the Lord for the person who makes a mistake") — the priestly atonement is the intercession made before God. The seeking operates through institutional means (prescribed offering) rather than through verbal petition, though Lev 5:5's confession ("confesses the sin he has committed") carries a supplication dimension — the verbal acknowledgment before God as part of the seeking process.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The supplication dimension is evidenced in the post-confession plea. 2Sa 24:10 — "please take away (*na*) the iniquity of your servant" — *na* (please, I pray) is the supplication particle attached to the plea that follows confession. Num 21:7 — "pray (*hitpallel*) to the Lord that he take away the serpents" — the confession generates a petition for intercession. Dan 9:17–19 — extended supplication following confession: "O Lord, hear (*shema*); O Lord, forgive (*selach*); O Lord, pay attention and act." The supplication vocabulary (na, hitpallel, shema, selach) appears consistently after confession, confirming that confession's natural completion is petition — the confession opens the space for plea.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(S)*

**[CHAR-4]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with conscience suppression. The conscience-suppressed person does not seek God through the suppression; the suppression is precisely the condition of not seeking. The absence of seeking-vocabulary is consistent with the characteristic's nature: the person who suppresses conscience does not know they need to seek anything — they believe all is well.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(S)*

**[CHAR-5]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with refusal to repent. The characteristic is the condition of one who is not seeking God; the seeking-vocabulary belongs to its opposite (shuv — returning to God). The absence of seeking-vocabulary is consistent with the characteristic's nature: the person who refuses to repent is precisely the person who is not seeking the relational restoration that repentance would initiate.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Limited evidence. 1Ki 8:46's prayer context — "if they sin against you... and you give them to an enemy... if they repent... and pray to you in the land of their captivity" — contains the prayer-seeking vocabulary (*hitpallel* — to pray/intercede) as part of the restoration path Solomon envisions. The seeking term belongs to the restoration sequence that follows the recognition of habitual sinning, not to the habitual sinning itself. The sinner-type vocabulary (hachotet) contains no seeking dimension; seeking belongs to the exit from the habitual pattern.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-7]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with contagious sin. The characteristic is a leader's causing action; the seeking-vocabulary belongs to the restoration movement that counters contagious sin (1Ki 8:46's prayer context presupposes contagious sin's consequences but belongs to the broader restoration framework). No seeking-vocabulary appears in the 19-verse corpus itself.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with political revolt. Political revolt is a power-assertion act, not a seeking act. The characteristic has no seeking dimension within the corpus.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(S)*

**[CHAR-9]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with sinful speech. The characteristic is a speech-act of harm; no seeking-of-sinful-speech vocabulary appears.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — The corpus contains no supplication vocabulary in connection with any of the three mechanisms. The characteristic names sinful pathways; no seeking-of-these-mechanisms vocabulary appears.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Yes — the VCG-02 redemption register contains extensive supplication and seeking vocabulary associated with addressing the condition. Act 3:19 — "repent... and turn back, that your sins may be blotted out" — repentance is the seeking-response; Luk 11:4 — "forgive us our sins" — the prayer-petition for forgiveness names the supplication vocabulary; Act 22:16 — "calling on his name" — the calling-on is the seeking act that accompanies the washing of sins.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Yes — in the intercessory context. 1Jo 5:16 — "he shall ask, and God will give him life" (*erōtēsei, kai dōsei autō zōēn*) — the asking (*erōtēsei*) is an intercessory petition directed toward God for the sinning brother; the prayer is the seeking act that addresses the enslaving power's hold on the brother. This is the only explicit supplication vocabulary in the 9-verse corpus.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Yes — the forgiveness-petition vocabulary. Exo 10:17 — "forgive my sin, please" (*sĕʾ-nāʾ ḥaṭṭāṯî*); Exo 34:9 — "pardon our iniquity and our sin" (*wĕsālaḥtā laʿāwōnênû ûlĕḥaṭṭāʾṯênû*); Psa 25:7 — "remember not the sins of my youth" — the petitionary vocabulary of seeking record-erasure from God is extensive throughout the corpus. The supplication-seeking is directed toward the removal of the record rather than toward receiving a quality.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — Exo 32:30 — "perhaps I can make atonement for your sin" (*ûlây ăkappərâ bəʿad ḥaṭṭaṯkem*) — the "perhaps" (*ûlây*) is the seeking-term of uncertainty; Moses's going up to the Lord is the seeking-act itself. Act 2:38 — "repent" (*metanoeō*) — the NT seeking-term; Act 10:43 — "everyone who believes in him" (*pas ho pisteuōn eis auton*) — the believing is the faith-seeking act. The supplication vocabulary is embedded in the seeking-actions rather than in dedicated petition-terms.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes — within the corporate confession context. 1Ki 8:47 — "repent (*nichāmû*) and plead (*hitchannĕnû*) with you" — the pleading (*hithchannēn* — to seek grace, to implore favour) is the supplication-term embedded in the seeking-movement toward God. The corporate pleading is the characteristic's seeking-posture: the community under the generational condition implores divine favour for the relief of the accumulated burden.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — within the self-recognition texts. Luk 18:13 — "God, be merciful to me, a sinner" (*hilasthēti moi tō hamartōlō*) — *hilasthēti* (be propitious/merciful) is the supplication-term; the sinner-identity is the explicit basis of the plea. The supplication is enabled by the self-recognition of the sinner-identity: "to me, the sinner" frames the identity as the ground of the appeal. Psa 25:8 — "he instructs sinners (*chat.ta.im*) in the way" — while not a supplication itself, implies that sinners are receptive to divine instruction when they accept the identity.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — within the guilt-acknowledgment-seeking complex. Hos 5:15 — "until they acknowledge their guilt and seek my face, and in their distress earnestly seek me" — *bāqaš* (to seek) paired with *ḥālāh* (to earnestly seek/implore) are the supplication-seeking terms following guilt-acknowledgment. Ezr 9:6's prayer posture — "I am ashamed and blush to lift my face to you, my God" — is itself a supplication-posture though not using a specific supplication-term. The seeking that follows guilt-acknowledgment is the characteristic's embedded supplication-direction.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Yes — within the iniquity-removal plea complex. Hos 14:2 — "take away all iniquity" (*śāʾ kol-ʿāwōn*) — the specific plea for iniquity-removal uses *śāʾ* (to lift, to take away); Psa 25:11 — "pardon my guilt" (*sālaḥtā laʿăwōnî* — "forgive my iniquity"); Exo 34:9 — "pardon our iniquity" — the supplication vocabulary for iniquity-removal consistently uses lifting/forgiving terms applied directly to a.von. The supplication is for removal of what CHAR-18 names.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes. Psa 51:1 — "blot out my transgressions" — the specific plea using *māḥāh* (to blot out, erase); Isa 43:25 — God's own use of the same term — "I blot out your transgressions for my own sake"; Psa 103:12 — "so far does he remove our transgressions from us" — *rāḥaq* (to remove far off) as the metaphor of spatial-relational distance. The supplication vocabulary for transgression-resolution is specifically erasure/blotting-out and removal — stronger than mere covering.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — within the confession complex. Lev 26:40 — "if they confess (*hithwaddû*) their iniquity and... their treachery" — the Hithpael of *yadah* (to confess, to acknowledge) is the supplication-verb preceding the covenant-remembering; Ezr 10:2 — "we have broken faith with our God" — the verbal acknowledgment is the seeking-term. The seeking is not of abstract forgiveness but specifically of covenant-remembering: Lev 26:42 — "I will remember my covenant" — the confession of treachery seeks the reactivation of the divine covenant-memory.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — within the confession-prayer register. 1Ki 8:47 — "acting perversely... we have sinned" — the full confession-prayer including perversity-acknowledgment; Dan 9:5 — "we have sinned and done wrong" — Daniel's seeking-prayer names the perversity. The supplication is the naming of the perversity within prayer as the honest foundation of the God-turning. Psa 38:6's "bowed down and prostrate" is the bodily supplication-posture under the weight of perversion's consequences.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — within the victim's plea register. Job 6:29 — "please turn; let no injustice be done" — the direct plea that no injustice be enacted; Psa 43:1 — "vindicate me, O God, and defend my cause against an ungodly people, from the deceitful and unjust man deliver me" — the plea for deliverance from the unjust as a seeking-term; Job 22:23 — "if you return to the Almighty you will be built up; if you remove injustice far from your tents" — Eliphaz's condition framing removal of injustice as the human seeking-act.

---

### T7.1.8

**Question:** What does the relationship between the OT Hebrew vocabulary and the NT Greek vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The continuity is striking: cha.ta (H2398) and hamartanō (G0264) share the same "miss the mark" root semantic and the same grammatical category (verb). The LXX translates cha.ta with hamartanō throughout, establishing lexical continuity. The development visible in the NT corpus (VCG-05) is not in the basic definition but in the relational theology: sinning against "your brother" (Mat 18:15) and "against Christ" (1Cor 8:12) extends the relational scope of sinning into the Christological register — a development not present in the OT corpus. The NT also introduces the advocate (1Jo 2:1) as the specific remedy for sinning, which reflects development in the understanding of how sinning is addressed even as the act-definition remains constant.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(S)*

**[CHAR-2]** S — The 18-verse CHAR-2 corpus is entirely from the OT (Hebrew Lev, Num, 1Sa, Exo, Eze). No NT Greek verses appear in the CHAR-2 corpus. The programme has not identified NT verses addressing unintentional sinning in the specific sense defined by CHAR-2. This absence itself is analytically noteworthy: the NT corpus does not carry the *bishgagah* (unintentional qualifier) vocabulary. The NT addresses sin broadly (hamartanō, hamartia) without the OT's systematic distinction between intentional and unintentional sinning through distinct remedial institutions. The relationship across Testaments is one of OT specification (CHAR-2 as a distinct category with a distinct remedy) and NT generalisation (sin addressed through Christ without the Levitical bifurcation).

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The OT-NT vocabulary relationship shows *continuity of structure, extension of scope*. Both Testaments use the sinning-vocabulary self-applied in the first person (*chatati* / *hēmarton*) as the confession form — no new confession-vocabulary is introduced in the NT. The extension: NT confessions include "against heaven" (Luk 15:18/21) — the cosmic dimension of sin named explicitly; and "by betraying innocent blood" (Mat 27:4) — specific act named with legal precision. The NT corpus is smaller within CHAR-3 (3 verses vs 31 OT) reflecting that the NT's confession theology is concentrated in its broader epistolary and narrative context rather than in the corpus's 34 verses. The shared form (*chatati*/*hēmarton*) across Testaments confirms that confession is not a NT innovation but a continuous theological practice.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Continuity of structure, development of context. Both Testaments use the sinning vocabulary in suppressed or denied mode: Jer 2:35 (OT — direct denial), 1Cor 15:34/Tit 3:11 (NT — ongoing suppression in community context). The OT instances show conscience suppression in the covenantal-prophetic confrontation mode: God speaks through the prophet to the denying people. The NT instances show it in the community-pastoral mode: Paul rebukes believers who continue sinning in spiritual stupor (1Cor 15:34) and describes the divisive person's deformed character (Tit 3:11). The development: the NT more explicitly describes the *cognitive* dimension ("no knowledge of God" — 1Cor 15:34) and the *character-deformation* consequence ("warped" — Tit 3:11) that the OT implies through narrative and prophetic confrontation.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The OT-NT vocabulary relationship shows continuity of concept with development of explicitness. The OT corpus expresses refusal to repent primarily through repeated narrative predication (did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam) — showing the characteristic through cumulative historical example. The NT corpus names the inner quality directly: Heb 10:26's *hekousiōs* (deliberately/willingly) and 1Ti 5:20's persistent-sinning present participle capture the volitional intensity of the refusal more explicitly than the OT's negated movement verbs. The development: OT shows the characteristic through recurring historical pattern; NT names the volitional quality (deliberate, persistent) that the OT implies.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The OT-NT relationship shows *continuity with NT intensification*. The OT establishes: the person-type (hachotet — Ecclesiastes), the universal baseline (1Ki 8:46; Ecc 7:20), the iterative pattern (Exo 9:34), and the historical persistence (Hos 10:9). The NT intensifies through: (1) the continuous-present participle (1Jo 3:6 — hamartanōn — "keeps on sinning"), which captures the ongoing-state dimension more precisely than the OT's participial person-type; (2) the relational-epistemic consequence (1Jo 3:6 — "never seen or known him") — a christological framing of the habitual sinning's relational meaning that the OT implies through covenant-language but does not name christologically.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-7]** S — The 19-verse corpus is entirely OT Hebrew. No NT Greek verses appear in CHAR-7. The characteristic is expressed exclusively through the Hiphil causative of the Hebrew cha.ta root — a grammatical form without a direct NT Greek equivalent in this corpus. The absence of NT vocabulary may reflect that the NT addresses the contagious sin dynamic through different conceptual frameworks (e.g. leadership accountability for communities — references to shepherds, elders) rather than through the Hiphil causative of the sinning verb specifically. The relationship reveals: CHAR-7 is a characteristically OT-Hebrew theological construct with its distinctive Hiphil grammatical expression; the NT may carry the same relational dynamic in different vocabulary.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The 9-verse corpus is entirely OT Hebrew; no NT Greek vocabulary appears. The political-revolt register of pa.sha (H6586) is a specifically OT Hebrew construct for breaking political submission. Whether the NT addresses the same political dynamic through different vocabulary (e.g. in political contexts) is outside the scope of this 9-verse corpus. The OT-NT relationship cannot be characterised from within this corpus.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The exclusively NT Greek corpus (no OT Hebrew vocabulary in the 22 verses) reveals that CHAR-9 as a conceptually unified characteristic is a distinctly NT formation. The OT does have verbal sin (e.g. Lev 24:11/16 — the blasphemer's stoning; 2Ki 19:6 — "blasphemed me" in the Assyrian context), but those OT instances use different Hebrew roots (gadaph, na.ats, na.aqab) and are not included in this corpus. The NT coheres around the single blasfēmia/blasfēmos word-family in a way the OT does not — the characteristic is thus more definitionally precise in NT Greek than its conceptual antecedents in OT Hebrew would allow.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The exclusively NT Greek corpus (no OT Hebrew vocabulary in the 5 verses) reveals that CHAR-10 as a conceptually organised grouping is entirely NT in its vocabulary, despite the mechanisms having conceptual antecedents in the OT (desire-exploitation in the Eden narrative; covenantal departure throughout the prophets). The NT vocabulary of deleazō/sunupokrinomai/apostasia gives these mechanisms specific names not found in OT Hebrew — the NT period is when these three mechanisms are named as distinct conceptual categories. This confirms the characteristic's NT-conceptual configuration.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The OT-NT vocabulary relationship reveals a *development from condition-as-character (OT) to condition-as-cosmic-power (NT)*. The OT vocabulary (rish.ah — Mal 1:4; al.vah — Hos 10:9; chat.tat — Gen 4:7) primarily names the condition as a settled inner wickedness-quality and as a predatory entity. The NT vocabulary (hamartia in Romans 5-8) develops the condition into a cosmic governing power (sin reigned, sin's law, sin and death as paired cosmic forces). The NT also adds the redemption register (VCG-02) that is absent from VCG-01's OT vocabulary. The development: OT — condition as quality and predatory entity → NT — condition as cosmic enslaving power that requires the incarnation's condemnation (Rom 8:3) and the resurrection's defeat (1Cor 15:17) to overcome.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The exclusively NT Greek corpus (no OT Hebrew vocabulary in the 9 verses) reveals that the enslaving-power characteristic as a conceptually focused analytical register is a NT development. The OT provides the conceptual background (Gen 4:7 — sin crouching, desiring mastery — in CHAR-11's corpus), but the specific noun-as-master constructions (Joh 8:34 — *doulos tēs hamartias*; Gal 3:22 — *hypo hamartian*) and the present-participle practice-grammar (1Jo 3:8/9) are NT-specific formulations. The NT develops the OT's sin-as-predator into sin-as-constituted-master with the full slave-ownership vocabulary of the Greco-Roman world.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The vocabulary relationship reveals *continuity of the divine-record concept from OT to NT with eschatological resolution in the NT*. The OT develops the record through five term-groups (chat.tat, cha.ta.ah, chet, cha.ta verb, and the erasure vocabulary) across 203 of the 205 verses. The NT (Joh 1:29; Rom 3:25) deploys the equivalent Greek vocabulary (hamartia, hamartēma) to name the OT record's eschatological resolution: Joh 1:29 — "the Lamb of God who takes away the sin of the world" — the world's accumulated OT record is the burden the Lamb removes; Rom 3:25 — "former sins" passed over — the OT record's unresolved account is now addressed through Christ's propitiation.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The relationship reveals *progressive mechanism-clarification*: the OT vocabulary (kip.pu.rim — covering; chat.tat — sin-offering) describes the mechanism institutionally without naming its ultimate ground; the NT vocabulary (hamartia aphesis — forgiveness of sins; "through his blood" — Eph 1:7; "blood of the covenant" — Mat 26:28) names the mechanism's ground (Christ's blood) and the institution's fulfillment. The OT system points forward through its provisional character (Exo 30:10 — "once in the year throughout your generations"); the NT declares the once-for-all fulfillment (Mat 26:28 — poured out "for many"). The relationship is type-to-antitype: OT covering mechanism → NT blood-atonement fulfillment.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-15]** S — The 8-verse corpus contains no NT Greek vocabulary. The characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew (8 verses, all cha.ta). The absence of NT vocabulary in this specific corpus does not mean generational sin is absent from the NT but that the programme's Phase 9 corpus for CHAR-15 draws exclusively from OT Hebrew sources. The OT-only corpus is consistent with the characteristic's primary locus in the Israel-covenant framework where generational sin is most explicitly addressed through ancestral-lineage language.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The relationship reveals *continuity with expansion*. Chat.ta (H2400 — OT) is used primarily for extreme-type sinners (Gen 13:13; 1Sa 15:18; Amo 9:8/10) and collective characterisations (Psa 1:1/5; Isa 1:28/13:9/33:14). Hamartōlos (G0268 — NT) expands the range to include social category (Mat 9:10/11; Luk 7:37), self-recognised identity (Luk 5:8; Luk 18:13), and constituted federal status (Rom 5:19). The NT expansion adds: the social-stigma register (VCG-01 — 20+ NT verses in social settings), the self-aware sinner's plea (VCG-01 — Luk 5:8; Luk 18:13), and the Adamic-constitution register (VCG-02 — Rom 5:19). The OT establishes the extreme-type and collective categories; the NT adds the individual self-aware and constituted registers.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(S)*

**[CHAR-17]** S — The 97-verse corpus contains no NT Greek vocabulary. The characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew. The guilt-realisation vocabulary (*ʾāšam* family) is distinctively OT in its priestly-ritual framing (Lev 4/5/6 sequences) and its communal accumulation language (Ezr 9:6/7/13/15). The NT equivalent vocabulary (conscience — *syneidēsis*; guilt-consciousness) does not appear in this corpus. The OT-only character reflects the characteristic's primary locus in the Levitical ritual framework and the historical-confessional tradition of the exilic period.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(S)*

**[CHAR-18]** S — The corpus contains no NT Greek vocabulary. The characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew. The a.von H5771G crime-register does not directly correspond to a single NT Greek term; NT equivalents (anomia — lawlessness; adikia — unrighteousness; hamartia — sin) distribute the semantic field differently. The absence of NT vocabulary in this corpus reflects the OT nature of the iniquity-as-crime concept in its full development. The Isa 53:5/6/11 servant-bearing passages are the theological bridge toward NT soteriology but use OT a.von terminology exclusively.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The OT/NT relationship reveals *continuity with legal precision*. The OT pe.sha/pa.sha rebellion-vocabulary (relational, covenantal, political) is conceptually continuous with the NT parabasis-vocabulary (legal, boundary-specific). Rom 4:15 — "where there is no law there is no transgression" — makes the parabasis-definition explicit: what the OT pe.sha named relationally, the NT parabasis names legally. The OT term covers rebellion broadly (covenant, relationship, authority); the NT term specifies the law-known-boundary-crossing structure. The paraptōma (VCG-05) adds the stumbling-aside dimension that the OT terms do not directly name. The NT vocabulary clarifies and specifies what the OT vocabulary named more broadly.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-20]** S — The corpus contains no NT vocabulary. The characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew. The ma.al/ba.gad vocabulary for covenant-faithlessness does not have direct NT Greek equivalents within the programme's studied corpus; NT equivalents would fall into M13 (faithfulness) or M31 (faith/unbelief) territory rather than M10. The OT-only character reflects the specific covenantal-relational framework of the Levitical, Deuteronomic, and prophetic traditions in which faithlessness is most precisely named. The absence of NT vocabulary is analytically significant: CHAR-20 is the M10 characteristic whose conceptual framework is most thoroughly OT-covenantal.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The OT/NT relationship reveals *continuity with expansion into the cosmic and desire dimensions*. The OT terms (tah.pu.khah, a.vah, te.vel, sa.laph) address perversion primarily at the level of acts, speech, judgment, and specific category-violations. The NT ftheirō/fthora vocabulary expands this into: the desire as the active corruption-agent (Eph 4:22; 2Pe 1:4), the cosmic scope (Rom 8:21 — creation's bondage), the mind-specific depravity (1Ti 6:5), and the eschatological judgment (Rev 19:2). The NT adds the mechanistic precision (desire-driven) and the cosmic scope that the OT terms do not independently provide.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The OT/NT relationship reveals *conceptual continuity with legal-forensic precision in the NT*. The OT a.vel/av.lah vocabulary covers injustice broadly: judicial, commercial, violent, verbal. The NT adikos/adikēma vocabulary focuses on: the person-type (adikos — the unjust as a moral category) and the legal-actionable wrong (adikēma — the demonstrable crime). Rom 3:5 — "that God is unrighteous to inflict wrath" — uses adikos in a precisely theological-forensic argument. 1Pe 3:18 — "the righteous for the unrighteous" — deploys adikos in the Christological substitution context. The NT narrows and sharpens the OT's broader injustice vocabulary toward theological and forensic precision.

---

### T7.1.9

**Question:** Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Paraptōma (G3900) represents a partially new NT deployment — "false step, trespass" — that emphasises the element of surprise or entanglement in sinning (Gal 6:1: "caught in any transgression"). This framing of sinning as something a person can be "caught in" introduces a nuance — sinning as a moral entanglement that the community must address through gentle restoration — that is less prominent in the OT cha.ta corpus. Hamartanō itself is not new (it is the LXX rendering of cha.ta) but its deployment in the 1 John corpus ("I am writing these things so that you may not sin," 1Jo 2:1) with the advocacy framework reflects a theological development in the understanding of how the sinning will is addressed under the new covenant.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(S)*

**[CHAR-2]** S — No NT term for unintentional sinning is present in the CHAR-2 corpus, and the programme has not identified a newly coined NT term for this specific characteristic. The silence is consistent with T7.1.8: the NT does not reproduce the OT's systematic intentional/unintentional distinction as a structural feature of its sin vocabulary.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(S)*

**[CHAR-3]** S — No newly coined NT term for confession appears in the 34-verse CHAR-3 corpus. The NT instances (Mat 27:4; Luk 15:18/21) use the existing hamartanō vocabulary in confessional mode. The NT does use *exomologeō* (to confess openly) and *homologeō* (to confess/acknowledge) in other contexts not in this corpus — those terms represent NT confessional vocabulary development, but they do not appear in the CHAR-3 34-verse corpus.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(S)*

**[CHAR-4]** S — No newly coined NT term for conscience suppression appears in the 8-verse corpus. The NT uses existing sinning vocabulary (hamartanō) in the ongoing-action and adjectival modes. The NT's *exestraptai* ("warped/distorted" — Tit 3:11) is a significant term for the deposit-consequence of conscience suppression but is not a dedicated conscience-suppression term; it describes the outcome rather than the mechanism.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — *Hekousiōs* (deliberately, willingly — Heb 10:26) applied to ongoing sinning after knowing the truth represents the NT's most explicit naming of the volitional quality of the characteristic. While not a newly coined term (the adverb was standard Greek), its deployment in Heb 10:26 in the context of deliberate post-knowledge sinning produces a specifically NT formulation: "sinning willingly after knowing the truth" is the NT's sharpest characterisation of what the OT's "did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam" implied throughout. The combination *hekousiōs* + *hamartanōn* + post-knowledge context is the NT's distinctive terminological contribution.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(S)*

**[CHAR-6]** S — No newly coined NT term for habitual defection appears in the 14-verse corpus. The NT uses the established hamartanō vocabulary in the continuous-present configuration (1Jo 3:6) — the continuous mode is not a new term but a grammatical configuration of the existing sinning vocabulary. 1Jo 3:6's *menōn* (abiding — the structural opposite) is not a new term but a standard NT presence-term used in its relational-theological sense by John.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-7]** S — No NT term for contagious sin appears in the 19-verse corpus. The characteristic is addressed through the OT Hiphil causative construct exclusively in this corpus. Whether the NT coins a term for the leadership-transmitted sinning dynamic in its own vocabulary is outside the scope of the 19-verse evidence base.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — No NT vocabulary appears in the 9-verse corpus. This question cannot be addressed from the corpus evidence.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Blasfēmia and blasfēmos were existing Greek terms before the NT — not NT coinages. However, the NT deploys them with a precision and scope that extends beyond classical Greek usage (where blasphemein could mean speaking ill of anyone). The NT systematically applies the term to both God-directed speech (divine authority usurpation — VCG-02/03) and person-directed speech (slander — VCG-01) within a single unified moral-theological framework. This unified deployment is a NT conceptual development even if not a lexical coinage.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Sunupokrinomai (G4942) appears to be specifically NT Greek — the compound *sun-hupokrinomai* is not attested in classical Greek in this form, making it a NT-period compound that names the specifically collective dimension of hypocrisy. The *sun-* prefix captures a conceptual innovation: not merely individual hypocrisy (*hupokrinomai*) but the distinctly collective act of joining in hypocrisy alongside others — a specific social mechanism named by this NT compound. This coinage reveals: the NT writers perceived collective complicity in hypocrisy as a distinct moral phenomenon requiring its own vocabulary.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The *personification of hamartia* as a subject with agency (Rom 5:21 — "sin reigned"; Rom 7:8 — "sin, seizing an opportunity") is a NT conceptual deployment that extends beyond OT precedents. While hamartia is a pre-NT Greek term, its sustained personification as a cosmic governing power in Romans 5-8 represents a NT conceptual innovation: sin is not merely named as a human failing but addressed as an entity with its own governing authority, desire (Gen 4:7 — "its desire is for you"), and employment relationship (Rom 6:23 — paying wages). This personification-grammar is the NT's distinctive contribution to the semantic range of the condition.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The construction *doulos tēs hamartias* (Joh 8:34 — slave of sin) — while not a newly coined single word — represents a NT conceptual formulation that applies the Greco-Roman slave-ownership vocabulary (*doulos* — chattel slave, not a hired servant) to the person's relationship to sin. The use of *doulos* (chattel slave) rather than *diakonos* (servant) or *therapōn* (attendant) names the most absolute form of human legal unfreedom — the enslaved person as property. This is a NT conceptual innovation: sin as a legal owner whose property the enslaved person is. The *doulos* vocabulary applied to sin-mastery appears not to have OT precedent in this form.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The collective singular "the sin of the world" (Joh 1:29 — *hē hamartia tou kosmou*) represents a NT deployment that gathers the entire accumulated OT-and-human divine record into a single noun-entity borne by the Lamb. While not a newly coined term, the *totalization* of the sin-record into a single collective entity ("the sin of the world") is a NT conceptual move: all individual, corporate, and generational records are gathered into one burden that the Lamb bears. This totalization metaphor is the NT's distinctive contribution to the characteristic's vocabulary.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — *Aphesis* (G0859 — forgiveness, release) is the specific NT term for the release/forgiveness granted — "the forgiveness of sins" (*aphesin tōn hamartiōn* — Mat 26:28; Act 2:38; Col 1:14). While aphesis has classical Greek precedent (release, pardon), its specific deployment for the divine act of sin-forgiveness through Christ's name is a NT development. The term names the receiving-side of the characteristic from the divine perspective: God's releasing of the sin-debt. Aphesis crystallises what kip.pu.rim described institutionally into a single declarative release-term.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-15]** S — No NT vocabulary appears in the 8-verse corpus. Whether NT terms for generational or inherited sinning exist cannot be determined from this corpus. Noted.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Hamartōlos itself is not newly coined in the NT but receives a distinctive NT deployment in the social-stigma register — "tax collectors and sinners" (*hamartōloi*) as a fixed social pairing (Mat 9:10/11; Mar 2:15/16; Luk 5:30/7:34/15:1/2) — which reflects the first-century Jewish social-moral classification system. The phrase "friend of sinners" (*philos hamartōlōn* — Mat 11:19; Luk 7:34) is used polemically against Jesus and represents a NT social context not paralleled in the OT chat.ta usage.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(S)*

**[CHAR-17]** S — No NT vocabulary appears in the corpus. Whether NT-period coinage for guilt-as-inner-state exists cannot be determined from this corpus. Noted.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(S)*

**[CHAR-18]** S — No NT vocabulary appears in the corpus. This prompt cannot be addressed from the available verse-evidence. Noted.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Parabasis (G3847) and parabatēs (G3848) are the NT's distinctive terminological contribution to CHAR-19: the parabasis-family appears in the NT in technical theological usage (Rom 4:15; Gal 3:19; Heb 2:2/9:15; Jam 2:9/11) and names the legally-precise transgression that could only exist where law had been promulgated. Rom 4:15's definitional statement — "where there is no law there is no transgression" — appears to be a distinctly NT theological articulation of the parabasis concept, not replicated in the OT in this explicit form.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(S)*

**[CHAR-20]** S — No NT vocabulary appears in the corpus. The characteristic's conceptual content in the NT period would be addressed through the M13 and M31 clusters rather than through M10-specific vocabulary. Noted.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — *Diaftheirō (G1311)* — "to corrupt thoroughly, to destroy completely" — appears in 1Ti 6:5 and 2Cor 4:16 as the intensified form of ftheirō, naming a complete or thoroughgoing corruption/destruction. The dia- prefix intensifies: where ftheirō names corruption, diaftheirō names the thorough-through corruption that penetrates comprehensively. 1Ti 6:5's "depraved in mind" (*noenous diephtharmenōn* — minds thoroughly corrupted) uses diaftheirō's intensified form to describe the mind-specific comprehensive depravity.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — *Adikēma (G0092)* — "wrongdoing, crime, injustice" — is the NT's distinctive contribution: the act-noun that names the specific legally-actionable deed of injustice rather than the character or condition. Act 18:14 — "if it were a matter of wrongdoing (*adikēma*) or vicious crime" — frames the term within Roman legal procedure; Rev 18:5 — "God has remembered her iniquities (*adikēmata*)" — deploys the term for cosmic-scale accumulated wrongdoings. The *adikēma* term frames injustice as a legally demonstrable, juridically accountable wrong — a precision the OT vocabulary does not provide with this specificity.

---

### T7.1.10

**Question:** What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The full vocabulary arc — from cha.ta's physical "missing" (Judg 20:16) through its covenantal "sinning against God" (Eze 14:13), through the LXX hamartanō bridge, into the NT's "sin against your brother" (Mat 18:15) and "sin against your own body" (1Cor 6:18), to the paraptōma "entanglement" (Gal 6:1) — reveals that wilful sinning covers the entire relational map of the human person (God, others, self) across its full directional range (deliberate, entangled, sustained). The semantic arc also reveals that sinning is consistently treated as an *act with consequences* at every point — the vocabulary never becomes purely abstract or dispositional.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The vocabulary arc — cha.ta + bishgagah (inadvertent sinning), shegagah (error), a.von (iniquity borne, Lev 5:1), chat.ta.ah (sin offering, Lev 9:15), cha.ta Piel (to purify, VCG-02) — reveals that CHAR-2's semantic range covers the full sweep from the inadvertent act through the resulting moral condition to the institutional remedy and its application to sacred spaces. The arc is *act → condition → remedy → restoration*, each stage named by distinct vocabulary. The full arc also reveals the characteristic's confinement to the OT Hebrew context: the *bishgagah* qualifier and the Levitical remedial vocabulary are OT-specific, and the NT's absence from this corpus confirms that the characteristic is most precisely defined and evidenced within the Hebrew covenantal system.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The vocabulary arc — *chatati* (I have sinned: OT individual), *chatanu* (we have sinned: OT corporate), *yadat* (to know: cognitive ground of confession, 2Sa 19:20), the heart-struck idiom (2Sa 24:10: affective-conscience ground), *na* + *selach* (please + forgive: supplication following), *lo yizzaker* (not remembered: divine response, Eze 33:16), *hēmarton* (NT: I sinned, same semantic structure) — reveals that the confession vocabulary is not a dedicated lexical field but the sinning vocabulary deployed in the self-application mode. The arc covers: the cognitive-conscience base, the verbal act, the supplication that follows, and the divine response. Together they map the complete moral episode of which confession is the pivot: sin named (confessed) → mercy sought (plea) → record cancelled (forgiveness).

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The vocabulary arc — from *lo chatati* (I have not sinned — Jer 2:35: active denial) through *hachotet* (the sinner — Ecc 8:12: person-type) through *hamartanō* continuous (1Cor 15:34: ongoing action in stupor) to *exestraptai* (warped — Tit 3:11: deposit consequence) — reveals that conscience suppression is expressed at every stage of the sinning vocabulary: denial of the act, constitution of the type, continuation of the pattern, and deformation of character. The full arc maps the characteristic's progression: the suppression begins as a denial or absence (act-level) and ends as a deformation (character-level). The vocabulary across both Testaments uses the same root domain but captures different phases of the same progressive deterioration.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The vocabulary arc — from *lo sur min* (OT negated departure: he did not depart from) through *davaq* (clung to) through *qasha/sarar* (stiffened neck/stubborn shoulder) through *hamartanōn* continuous (NT persisting in sin) through *hekousiōs hamartanontōn* (NT sinning deliberately) — reveals that refusal to repent is expressed at every stage of the characteristic's development: the passive non-departure (lo sur), the active attachment (davaq), the constitutional hardening (qasha/sarar), the ongoing action (hamartanōn continuous), and the deliberate volitional choice against known truth (hekousiōs). The full arc maps the characteristic from its mildest (passive non-departure) to its most severe (deliberate volitional sinning after knowing truth), with the vocabulary itself tracking the progressive intensification.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The vocabulary arc — from universal sinning (1Ki 8:46; Ecc 7:20 — "no one who does not sin") through person-type (hachotet — the sinner as constituted type — Ecc 7:26; 9:2/18; Pro 11:31/13:22) through iterative pattern (Exo 9:34 — "sinned yet again and hardened") through historical-generational persistence (Hos 10:9 — from Gibeah continued) through communal destruction (Ecc 9:18 — one sinner destroys much good) through NT continuous-state (1Jo 3:6 — "keeps on sinning / never seen or known him") — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary tracks every stage of its development: universal susceptibility → individual person-type → iterative act → historical pattern → communal consequence → relational-epistemic verdict. The arc is the most comprehensive temporal sweep of any characteristic's vocabulary in the M10 corpus.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The vocabulary arc — Hiphil causative of cha.ta deployed across three mechanisms (authority: 1Ki 16:2; structural construction: Jer 32:35; teaching: Deu 20:18) through the full span of the Kings narrative from Jeroboam's initial transmission to Josiah's structural reversal (2Ki 23:15) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary is singular and precise: the Hiphil causative is CHAR-7's exclusive identifier. The arc covers: preventive naming (Exo 23:33; Deu 20:18), historical transmission (1Ki 14:16 through 2Ki 21:16), and structural reversal (2Ki 23:15). The vocabulary arc tracks the entire life of the Jeroboam pattern from prophetic warning through national consequence to physical dismantling — a ~300-year narrative horizon expressed through a single grammatical form.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The vocabulary arc — pa.sha exclusively, across all 9 verses, covering ongoing condition (1Ki 12:19), specific event (2Ki 1:1/3:5), military-politically significant occasion (2Ki 3:7), revolt-plus-independence-establishment (2Ki 8:20), and "to this day" permanent condition (2Ki 8:22; 2Ch 10:19; 2Ch 21:10) — reveals that CHAR-8 is the most vocabularily homogeneous characteristic in the M10 corpus. The exclusive use of a single root across 9 verses without any alternate vocabulary confirms that the characteristic is definitionally narrow: it is exactly what pa.sha names in its political register, nothing more and nothing less. The arc covers the full temporal range of the act: from specific event through ongoing condition to permanent fracture.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The vocabulary arc — from person-directed slander (VCG-01 — Eph 4:31/Col 3:8 — community context) through divine-authority usurpation (VCG-02 — Mat 12:31/Mar 2:7/Joh 10:33 — christological context) through constitutive anti-God defiance (VCG-03 — Rev 13:1/5/6/17:3 — eschatological context) — reveals that blasfēmia names a single unified semantic field: speech that harms by claiming, usurping, or defying what is not the speaker's to claim, usurp, or defy. The arc covers the full range from relational-interpersonal harm (slander) through theological-judicial claim (blasphemy) to cosmic-constitutive defiance (the beast). The unifying thread is harmful speech from inner corruption directed at relational targets (persons or God).

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The vocabulary arc — three distinct Greek terms (deleazō/bait-lure, sunupokrinomai/collective-acting, apostasia/standing-away-from) covering desire-exploitation (Jam 1:14; 2Pe 2:14/18), collective identity-performance (Gal 2:13), and covenantal-departure (Act 21:21) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary is diverse but united by a common analytical insight: these are the *specific inner mechanisms* through which sin operates. Each term names a precise mechanism rather than a general category of sinning. The arc reveals: the NT period was conceptually attentive to the *how* of sin — the specific pathways, processes, and mechanisms — in a way that produced dedicated vocabulary for three distinct sinning mechanisms.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The vocabulary arc — from chat.tat's crouching predator (Gen 4:7 — OT origin-point) through rish.ah's settled wickedness-identity (Isa 9:18; Mal 1:4/3:15/4:1) through hamartia's redemption register (VCG-02 — forgiveness declared) through hamartia's cosmic-power register (VCG-03 — enslaving power, indwelling force, governing law) — reveals the characteristic's complete semantic field: the universal condition as (1) a predatory entity desiring dominion, (2) a settled inner wickedness-character, (3) a debt requiring divine forgiveness, and (4) a cosmic enslaving power requiring the death and resurrection of Christ to defeat. The arc is the most comprehensive in M10: it spans the full range of how the condition is conceptualised from Genesis through Paul's systematic development in Romans.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The vocabulary arc — from hamartia-as-owner (Joh 8:34 — *doulos tēs hamartias*) through hamartia-as-imprisoner (Gal 3:22 — *hypo hamartian*) through hamartanō-as-practice (1Jo 3:8/9 — present participial forms) through the liberation-vocabulary (born of God — 1Jo 3:9; 5:18) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary maps the *full governance-cycle of sin's enslaving power*: sin owns (Joh 8:34), sin confines (Gal 3:22), sin is practised in continuous present (1Jo 3:8), and liberation comes through divine birth that constitutionally prevents the continuous sinning (1Jo 3:9). The arc is a complete theological grammar of enslavement and liberation.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The vocabulary arc — from chat.tat's individual act-as-fact (Lev 4:23 — "the sin which he has committed") through chet's personal-bearing (Num 9:13 — "bear his sin") through cha.ta.ah's heart-inscription (Jer 17:1 — "engraved on the tablet of their heart") through the storage/book metaphors (Hos 13:12 — "kept in store"; Exo 32:32 — "your book") through the erasure vocabulary (Isa 43:25 — blotted out; Mic 7:19 — cast into the sea) to Joh 1:29's totalization ("the sin of the world" lifted by the Lamb) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary maps the complete divine-record lifecycle: creation of the entry, personal bearing, internal inscription, divine storage, escalating consequences, and finally eschatological erasure.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The vocabulary arc — from kāpar's covering-mechanism (OT — the sin covered by sacrifice) through chat.tat's sin-offering institution (OT — the animal bearing the sin) through aphesis's divine release-declaration (NT — God releasing the debt) through hamartia's forgiveness-scope (NT — the sin of the world whose Lamb takes it away — Joh 1:29) through paraptōma's not-counted-trespass (NT — 2Cor 5:19) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary moves from institutional-mechanism (how atonement is performed) to declarative-event (the moment of divine release). The arc is: ritual institution → sacrificial mechanism → priestly covering → divine declaration of release → permanent redemption-identity.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The vocabulary arc — exclusively cha.ta (H2398) deployed across founding-ancestral (Isa 43:27 — "your first father sinned"), escalating-continuation (Psa 78:17 — "sinned still more"), persistent-unbelief (Psa 78:32 — "still sinned despite wonders"), prolonged-duration (Isa 64:5 — "a long time in our sins"), corporate-doom-cause (Jer 8:14 — "because we have sinned"), divine-rescue (Eze 37:23 — "backslidings in which they have sinned"), and eschatological-curse (Isa 65:20 — the sinner cursed) — reveals a characteristic whose entire vocabulary range is the temporal and contextual depth of the universal sinning verb. The arc covers the complete generational sequence: origin → escalation → prolonged condition → doom → confession → divine rescue → eschatological resolution, all through a single verb applied across the generational time-span.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The vocabulary arc — from chat.ta's intensive-agent characterisation of extreme sinners (OT — Gen 13:13; Sodom; Amalekites) through hamartōlos's social-stigma category (NT synoptic — tax collectors and sinners at table) through the self-aware sinner's plea (Luk 5:8; Luk 18:13) through the federal constitution of all as sinners (Rom 5:19 — "made sinners") through the foremost-sinner paradigm (1Ti 1:15) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary charts the complete moral-identity spectrum: from extreme habitual type (chat.ta — OT) to socially recognised category (hamartōlos — synoptic) to constituted universal standing (hamartōlos — Pauline) to self-aware individual identity (hamartōlos — Luk 5:8; 18:13). The arc covers every mode through which the sinner-identity can be held.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The vocabulary arc — from a.sham's guilt-recognition verb (Lev 5:5 — the conscience-engagement moment) through ash.mah's accumulated guilt-condition (Ezr 9:6 — mounting to the heavens) through a.von H5771H's transferable guilt-burden (Exo 28:38 — Aaron bearing Israel's guilt on his forehead) through a.von H5771I's punitive-consequence dimension (Gen 4:13 — punishment greater than Cain can bear; Eze 4:4/5/6 — prophet bearing Israel's punishment) through pu.qah's conscience-stagger (1Sa 25:31 — pangs of conscience) through ash.mah's indelibility (Jer 2:22 — stain before God despite soap) to Isa 6:7's removal (guilt taken away) — reveals a characteristic whose vocabulary covers the complete guilt-arc from its conscience-recognition origin through its accumulation and weight through its transference and punishment-bearing to its indelible divine-visibility and finally its removal by divine act.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The vocabulary arc — a single term (a.von H5771G) across 162 verses from Gen 15:16 (Amorites' iniquity not yet complete — measurability) through Exo 20:5 (generational transmission) through Lev 16:21/22 (priestly scapegoat ritual — transference) through Psa 38:4 (overwhelming weight) through Isa 53:5/6/11 (servant bearing the iniquity of all — typological climax) through Jer 31:34 (new covenant forgiveness — eschatological resolution) through Zec 3:9 (iniquity of the land removed in a single day) to Mal 2:6 (faithful priest turning many from iniquity) — reveals the full moral-crime arc of accumulated iniquity in OT theology: from its measurable accumulation (Gen 15:16) through its overwhelming weight (Psa 38:4) to its vicarious bearing by the servant (Isa 53) to its new covenant dissolution (Jer 31:34). The single-term consistency across the full arc confirms CHAR-18 as the OT's primary vocabulary for the objective moral-crime accumulation that culminates in the servant's bearing.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The full vocabulary arc — from pe.sha's relational rebellion (Gen 31:36 — interpersonal; Isa 1:2 — "they have rebelled against me") through sa.rah's inner defiance (Isa 1:5 — "whole head sick and whole heart faint" from continued revolt) through the Amos threshold-counting (Amos 1:3 — "three and four") through Isa 53:5/8's servant pierced for transgressions through Dan 9:24's appointed finish through parabasis's law-violation precision (Rom 4:15) through paraptōma's Adam-paradigm (Rom 5:15/16/17/18/20) — reveals the complete arc of deliberate boundary-crossing from its first instance (Adam's trespass) through its OT relational-rebellion register through its eschatological appointed finish (Dan 9:24) through its NT legal precision to its resolution through the servant's bearing.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*
