**[CHAR-20]** E — The vocabulary arc — from Exo 21:8 (ma.al — breaking faith with a designated woman) through Lev 5:15/6:2 (ma.al — cultic breach of faith against holy things and God) through Num 5:12/27 (ma.al/ba.gad — marital faithlessness ordeal) through Jos 7:1/22:16-31 (ma.al — covenant-breach of the ban; Achan-to-Israel) through Chronicles' sustained ma.al-history (1Ch 9:1/10:13; 2Ch 12:2/26:16/28:19/22/29:6/30:7/36:14) through Ezra's communal confession (ma.al — Ezr 9:2/4/10:2/6/10) through the Psalms/Proverbs ba.gad-portrait of the traitor (Psa 25:3/119:158; Pro 11:3/6/13:2/15) through Isaiah's constitutional-character treachery (Isa 21:2/24:16/33:1/48:8) through Jeremiah/Ezekiel's covenant-betrayal accusation (Jer 3:20/5:11; Eze 14:13/17:20/39:23) through Malachi's spirit-vigilance command (Mal 2:14/15/16) — reveals the complete OT covenantal-faithlessness arc from individual sacred-trespass through marital-covenant-breach through national covenant-betrayal to constitutional inner character.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The vocabulary arc — from sa.laph's judgment-subversion (Exo 23:8 — bribery overturning justice) through tah.pu.khah's speech-perversion (Pro 6:14 — perverted heart; Pro 8:13 — wisdom hating perverseness) through a.vah's personal moral-twisting (1Ki 8:47; Dan 9:5 — "acted perversely") through te.vel's created-order violation (Lev 18:23 — bestiality as perversion) through sur's degenerative vine-turning (Jer 2:21) through cha.val's destructive corruption (Neh 1:7; Isa 32:7) through ftheirō's progressive desire-corruption (Eph 4:22) through diaftheirō's comprehensive mind-depravity (1Ti 6:5) through fthora's cosmic bondage-to-corruption (Rom 8:21) to Rev 19:2's judgment of the cosmic corrupting agent — reveals M10's most terminologically diverse characteristic spanning all levels from individual judgment-perception through speech through personal moral-twisting through created-category-violation through cosmic decay to eschatological judgment.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The vocabulary arc — from a.vel's judicial prohibition (Lev 19:15 — "do no injustice in court") through av.lah's violent-oppression register (Psa 58:2 — "your hands deal out violence") through av.val's person-type naming (Job 27:7/29:17 — "the unrighteous") through adikos's NT person-type precision (1Cor 6:9 — "the unrighteous will not inherit"; 1Pe 3:18 — "the righteous for the unrighteous") through adikēma's legal-crime specificity (Act 18:14; Rev 18:5) — reveals the complete arc of injustice from its most concrete prohibitions (judicial, commercial) through its person-type constitution to its legal-theological precision and eschatological reckoning. The arc confirms CHAR-22 as M10's most institutionally specific characteristic and M26's direct antonym-partner.

---

## T7.2 — Verse and Literary Interpretation

### T7.2.1

**Question:** What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role does it play in the sentence and argument?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The primary anchor verse is Psa 51:4: "Against you, you only, have I sinned and done what is evil in your sight." Cha.ta functions here as the confessional predicate — the act that David names as the fundamental moral reality of his situation. The term functions syntactically as the verb of confession ("I have sinned"), semantically as the identifier of the act's relational target ("against you, you only"), and theologically as the premise of the entire psalm's petition for forgiveness. It locates the entire moral reality — adultery, murder, deception — within the single relational frame of sinning against God. The function is definitional: cha.ta names what all David's wrongs ultimately are in their deepest character.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The primary anchor verse is Lev 4:2: "If anyone sins unintentionally in any of the Lord's commandments about things not to be done, and does any one of them." Cha.ta + bishgagah functions here as the *conditional predicate* — defining the condition under which the entire sin-offering system's individual-person provisions apply. The term governs the entire Lev 4–5 case-law structure: the "if" + the sinning-unintentionally condition opens the legal gate through which the remedial provisions flow. The term is definitional and structural: it establishes the precise inner-quality criterion (inadvertence) that triggers this specific regulatory path rather than the high-handed path (Num 15:30).

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The primary anchor verse is 2Sa 24:10: "But David's heart struck him after he had numbered the people. And David said to the Lord, 'I have sinned greatly in what I have done. But now, O Lord, please take away the iniquity of your servant, for I have done very foolishly.'" *Chatati* ("I have sinned") functions as the *confessional predicate* — the moral verdict David applies to his own act. Within the sentence, it functions as the main verb of self-accusation, followed by two supporting characterisations ("greatly in what I have done," "done very foolishly") that specify the moral quality of the named sin. The term functions as both the legal confession (naming the act as culpable) and the relational acknowledgment (naming the act against the background of David's relationship with God).

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The primary anchor verse is Jer 2:35: "you say, 'I am innocent; surely his anger has turned from me.' Behold, I will bring you to judgment for saying, 'I have not sinned.'" The term *lo chatati* ("I have not sinned") functions as: (1) *the quoted speech-act* that God declares will be judged — it is the specific verbal act that constitutes the false innocence claim; (2) *the defining inversion of confession* — the term is the exact grammatical form of the confessional chatati (*I have sinned*), negated; (3) *the grounds of judgment* — the verse makes the speaking of this negation the specific basis for the announced judgment. The term functions simultaneously as speech-act, moral verdict, and the ground of divine accountability.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The primary anchor verse is 2Ki 3:3: "Nevertheless, he clung to the sin of Jeroboam the son of Nebat, which he made Israel to sin; he did not depart from it." The term *davaq* ("clung to") functions as: (1) the *predicate of attachment* — it names the volitional relationship between Jehoram and the sinful course as an active bonding; (2) *the intensifier* — "clung to" is stronger than the standard "did not depart from," signalling an advanced stage of the refusal; (3) *the structural contrast* within the verse — "nevertheless... clung" — despite the context of other reforms (Jehoram had removed the Baal pillar — 2Ki 3:2), the clinging persists; the contrast makes the refusal more visible.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The primary anchor verse is Hos 8:11: "Because Ephraim has multiplied altars for sinning, they have become to him altars for sinning." The primary term *cha.ta* (sinning — appearing twice in the verse) functions as: (1) the *purpose-marker* of the altars ("altars for sinning" — the sinning is the declared purpose of the multiplied altars, naming the habitual defection's institutional form); (2) the *productive-outcome* of the altars ("they have become to him altars for sinning" — the second occurrence names what the altars produce: more sinning). The term functions in a causal self-reinforcing structure: the sinning produces altars that produce sinning. The double appearance of the term in a single verse is unique in the corpus and evidences the self-reinforcing logic of habitual defection.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The primary anchor verse is 1Ki 16:2: "Since I exalted you out of the dust and made you leader over my people Israel, and you have walked in the way of Jeroboam and have made my people Israel to sin, provoking me to anger with their sins." The Hiphil causative *wayaḥṭē* ("made... to sin") functions as: (1) *the specific indictment term* — the precise charge God lays against the king is the causative sinning, not merely the personal sinning; (2) *the relational qualifier* — "my people" combined with the Hiphil shows God's continuing relational claim over the community the leader has corrupted; (3) *the causal chain marker* — "made... to sin, provoking me to anger with their sins" — the Hiphil is the causal link between the leader's action and God's provocation.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The primary anchor verse is 2Ch 21:10: "So Edom revolted from the rule of Judah to this day. At that time Libnah also revolted from his rule, because he had forsaken the Lord, the God of his fathers." Pa.sha functions as: (1) *the act-term naming the decisive political defection* ("revolted from the rule of"); (2) *the condition-term marking the permanent consequence* ("to this day" — the revolt is still in effect); (3) *the first element in a compound causal-consequential statement* — the revolt is named first, then its theological ground ("because he had forsaken the Lord") is given. The term thus functions as both the act-name and the consequence-marker within a theologically framed causal argument.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The primary anchor verse is Mat 15:19: "For out of the heart come evil thoughts, murder, adultery, sexual immorality, theft, false witness, *slander*." Blasfēmia functions as: (1) *the final named member* of the heart-generated evil list — placed at the end, giving it climactic weight; (2) *the speech-act representative* of the entire list — while murder, adultery, and theft are acts, slander is specifically the speech-act, confirming that sinful speech carries the same moral weight as the gravest acts; (3) *the diagnostic term* — slander's presence in the list confirms that the heart's corruption reaches all the way to the mouth, making speech the diagnostic site of inner moral condition.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The primary anchor verse is Jam 1:14: "But each person is tempted when he is lured and enticed by his own desire." Deleazō (in its two passive forms — "lured" and "enticed") functions as: (1) *the mechanism-naming term* — two near-synonymous terms for the enticement process, naming the progressive stages (lured first, then enticed — escalating engagement); (2) *the responsibility-locating term* — "his own desire" — the possessive confirms that the mechanism operates through the person's own inner faculty, not through purely external forces; (3) *the universalising anchor* — "each person" — the subject makes the mechanism applicable to all moral subjects. The term functions as the entry-point definition of the enticement mechanism.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The primary anchor verse is Rom 3:23: "for all have sinned and fall short of the glory of God." *Hamartanō* (verb — "have sinned") functions as the universalising past-tense verb that encompasses all moral subjects in a single completed-act framing: "all have sinned" is the conclusion of the universal-sinning indictment that begins in Rom 1. The complementary phrase "fall short of the glory of God" adds the definitional framework: the universality (all) + the act (have sinned) + the deficiency (fall short of) + the standard (glory of God) together constitute the most precise and comprehensive statement of the universal sin-condition in the entire 112-verse corpus.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The primary anchor verse is Joh 8:34: "everyone who practices sin is a slave to sin." Hamartia in the genitive (*tēs hamartias* — of sin) functions as the *owner-name*: "a slave of sin" — sin is named in the genitive as the master in whose possession the slave stands. The genitive of possession/ownership is the grammatical expression of the enslaving relationship: the person is sin's property. The subject (*pas ho poiōn tēn hamartian* — everyone who is making sin) names the universal scope of the mechanism: every practising sinner is sin's property.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The primary anchor is Jer 17:1: "The sin of Judah is written with a pen of iron; with a point of diamond it is engraved on the tablet of their heart, and on the horns of their altars." *Cha.ta.ah* (H2403A/H2401 — "the sin of Judah") functions as the subject of the writing metaphor: sin is the content being inscribed. The double-instrument metaphor (iron pen + diamond point) functions as an intensifier of permanence: the record is inscribed with the hardest possible writing tools, implying the inscription cannot be self-erased. The dual location (heart tablet + altar horns) functions to name the record's internal (heart — private inner register) and external (altars — public institutional register) locations simultaneously.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The primary anchor is Mat 26:28: "for this is my blood of the covenant, which is poured out for many for the forgiveness of sins." The primary terms function as: (1) *"blood of the covenant"* (*to haima mou tēs diathēkēs*) — the covenant-blood functions as the ground of forgiveness; the possessive ("my blood") identifies Christ as the one whose blood establishes the mechanism; (2) *"poured out for many"* (*to peri pollōn ekchunoumenon*) — the pouring-out (present passive participle) names the act of self-offering as the forgiveness-mechanism; (3) *"for the forgiveness of sins"* (*eis aphesin hamartiōn*) — the purpose clause names the telos: all of this is for the releasing of sins. The terms function together: mechanism (blood) + act (poured out) + scope (for many) + purpose (forgiveness).

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The primary anchor is Isa 43:27: "Your first father sinned, and your mediators transgressed against me." Cha.ta (*chāṭaʾ* — Qal perfect, third person masculine singular) functions as the *founding-attribution verb*: it attributes the first father's sinning as the origin-event of the present community's condition. The grammar is significant: the Qal perfect names a completed past act whose consequences are still operative ("your first father sinned" — the sinning is past and complete, but "your" indicates the present community still stands in the resulting condition). The parallel with "your mediators transgressed against me" establishes a two-stage chain: foundational ancestor + institutional reinforcers = the generational pattern.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Two primary anchors. (1) *Rom 5:19* — "by the one man's disobedience the many were made sinners (*hamartōloi katestathēsan*)" — *hamartōloi* functions as the predicate-complement of *katestathēsan* (were constituted/appointed); the passive voice names the sinner-identity as received rather than achieved; the aorist passive confirms a completed past constitution-event. (2) *Gen 13:13* — "the men of Sodom were wicked, great sinners (*chat.ta.im gĕdōlîm*) against the Lord" — *chat.ta.im gĕdōlîm* functions as the predicate characterisation: the agent-noun plural with the intensifier "great" describes the extreme extent of the totalised sinner-character.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Two primary anchors. (1) *Ezr 9:6* — "I am ashamed and blush to lift my face to you, my God, for our iniquities have risen higher than our heads, and our guilt (*ash.mah*) has mounted up to the heavens" — ash.mah functions as the subject of the mounting metaphor; the guilt-condition is named as the entity that has risen and accumulated; the verb "mounted up" (*gāḏlāh*) measures its quantity. The mounting metaphor names the accumulation dynamic of VCG-02 precisely. (2) *Lev 5:5* — "when he realizes his guilt (*yʾāšam*) in any of these and confesses the sin he has committed" — a.sham (Qal imperfect) functions as the guilt-recognition verb; its activation is the trigger for the confession that follows; the verb names the conscience's engagement event.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Three primary anchors. (1) *Gen 15:16* — "the iniquity of the Amorites is not yet complete" — a.von functions as the subject of the measurability statement; the iniquity is the entity being measured toward its threshold; the "not yet complete" (*lōʾ šālēm*) establishes the accumulation-toward-threshold mechanism. (2) *Eze 18:20* — "the wickedness of the wicked shall be upon himself" — a.von functions as the personal-attachment predicate; the iniquity is what adheres to the responsible individual. (3) *Isa 53:6* — "the Lord has laid on him the iniquity of us all" — a.von functions as the object of divine placement; the collective human iniquity is the content transferred to the servant.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Three primary anchors. (1) *Psa 36:1* — "transgression speaks to the wicked deep in his heart; there is no fear of God before his eyes" — pe.sha functions as the speaking-subject; transgression is personified as an inner voice that has taken up residence in the heart; the verse's key move is locating transgression as an internal counselling presence; (2) *Rom 4:15* — "where there is no law there is no transgression" — parabasis functions as the predicate of the conditional: law is the necessary precondition; the definitional structure reveals the law-boundary-crossing nature; (3) *Amos 1:3* — "for three transgressions of Damascus, and for four" — pe.sha functions as the counted moral-crimes of a nation; the counting formula reveals the threshold-measurement structure.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Three primary anchors. (1) *1Ch 10:13* — "Saul died for his breach of faith (*māʿal*). He broke faith (*māʿal*) with the Lord in that he did not keep the command of the Lord" — ma.al functions as both the verdict-noun ("for his breach of faith") and the accusation-verb ("He broke faith") in the same verse; the double use names the faithlessness as both the cause of death (juridical function) and the specific act (evidential function); (2) *Mal 2:14* — "though she is your companion and your wife by covenant" and "to whom you have been faithless (*bāgadtāh*)" — ba.gad functions as the predicate naming the faithlessness within the most intimate covenantal relationship; (3) *Isa 48:8* — "I knew that you would surely deal treacherously (*bāgôd tibgôd*)" — the infinitive absolute + finite verb construction (*bāgôd tibgôd*) intensifies the certainty and totality of the foreknown treachery; the doubling is the Hebrew grammar of absolute certainty.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Two primary anchors. (1) *Pro 6:14* — "with perverted heart (*tahpukôt*) devises evil, continually sowing discord" — tah.pu.khah functions as the qualifying adjective for the heart; the perverseness characterises the heart's operative condition from which the evil-devising proceeds; the heart's perverted state is the grammatical subject's inner characterisation; (2) *Eph 4:22* — "old self which belongs to your former manner of life and is corrupt (*phtheiromenos*) through deceitful desires" — ftheirō in the present passive participle ("being corrupted") functions as the active ongoing condition of the old self; the present tense confirms the progressive nature; the passive voice names the old self as the entity being acted upon by deceitful desires.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Three primary anchors. (1) *Psa 58:2* — "in your hearts you devise wrongs (*av.lah*); your hands deal out violence on earth" — av.lah functions as the object of the devising: it is what the heart schemes; its position as the scheming's content names it as the substance of the planned wrongdoing; (2) *Luk 16:10* — "one who is dishonest (*adikos*) in a very little is also dishonest (*adikos*) in much" — adikos functions as the character-predicate; its repetition in both clauses names dishonesty/injustice as the consistent character-property; (3) *Deu 32:4* — "without iniquity (*av.lah*), just and upright is he" — av.lah functions as the negated quality; its explicit negation establishes injustice as the standard-opposite of God's character.

---

### T7.2.2

**Question:** What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle, apocalyptic) — and what does that form require for responsible interpretation?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The corpus spans multiple literary forms. (1) *Psalm* (Psa 51:4; 39:1; 119:11; 41:4): devotional/confessional poetry requires reading the cha.ta usage within the psalmist's first-person relational posture — the sinning named is personally owned, not described from outside. (2) *Narrative* (Gen 20:6/9/39:9; Jos 7:20; 1Sa 15:24; 2Sa 12:13): responsible interpretation requires attention to the narrator's moral framing — whether sinning is affirmed, denied, or corrected within the narrative logic. (3) *Prophetic oracle* (Eze 18:4/20; 14:13; Hos 4:7; 13:2; Isa 1:4): prophetic sinning-language requires reading within the covenant-warning and judgment frame — sinning here is declared as the cause of coming or present judgment. (4) *Levitical law* (Lev 4/5 — VCG-04): casuistic law requires reading sinning as a legally defined category triggering prescribed obligations.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The dominant literary form is **casuistic law** (case-law: "if anyone... then...") in Lev 4–6 and Num 15:27/28. Responsible interpretation of casuistic law requires: (1) reading the case as a *legal category* rather than a narrative — the verse establishes a rule for a type-scenario, not a single historical event; (2) recognising that the inner-quality qualifier (*bishgagah*) is legally operative — it functions as the determining condition that routes the case to this remedial track; (3) not reading inner-being content beyond what the legal text specifies. The secondary literary form is **narrative** (Num 22:34 — Balaam's confession; 1Sa 14:33/34 — Saul's response to the people's violation), which requires attention to the narrative's specific moral evaluation of the character's act.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The corpus spans four literary forms. (1) *Narrative* (2Sa 24:10/17; 1Ch 21:8/17; Exo 9:27/10:16; 1Sa 15:30/26:21; Luk 15:18/21; Mat 27:4): confession embedded in narrative requires attention to the narrative's moral evaluation — whether the confession is confirmed as genuine by what follows (David's — confirmed; Pharaoh's — reversed; Saul's — ambiguous). (2) *Prayer/psalm* (Neh 1:6; Dan 9:5/8/11/15; Psa 106:6): extended prayer-confession requires reading the full prayer context to understand what the confession is oriented toward. (3) *Prophetic oracle* (Jer 3:25; 14:7/20; Mic 7:9): prophetically-framed confessions require reading within the covenant-warning or restoration context. (4) *Lament* (Lam 5:7): confession embedded in communal lament requires reading the affective and communal dimensions carefully — the lament is not merely penitential but also an appeal for divine attention.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Prophetic oracle* (Jer 2:35; 16:10): God speaks through Jeremiah; requires reading within the covenant-confrontation genre — the prophet delivers God's direct response to the people's moral condition. (2) *Wisdom observation* (Ecc 2:26; 8:12): Qohelet observes the sinner as a general type; requires reading within the wisdom genre's reflective-observational mode — the sinner is presented as a cautionary human type, not addressed directly. (3) *Apostolic rebuke* (1Cor 15:34): Paul directly addresses the community; requires reading within the epistle genre's corrective and exhortational mode. (4) *Pastoral instruction* (Tit 3:11): Paul instructs Titus on handling a divisive person; requires reading within the pastoral-instruction genre — a practical community-management text with embedded theological characterisation.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The dominant literary form is **regnal narrative** (the formulaic evaluation of kings in Kings and Chronicles): each king's reign is evaluated in the same formula — "he did evil in the sight of the Lord; he did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam." Responsible interpretation of regnal narrative requires: (1) reading the formula as a theological verdict, not merely historical observation — the narrator applies a moral-theological evaluation to each reign; (2) recognising the cumulative effect of the repeated formula — it is not a one-off judgment but an accumulating testimony; (3) understanding the Jeroboam reference as a theological benchmark — Jeroboam's sin (golden calves at Bethel and Dan — 1Ki 12:28–33) is the canonical example of syncretistic idolatry that became the measuring stick for Israel's kings. Secondary forms: *prayer/psalm* (Neh 9:29 — communal confessional prayer requiring reading within the prayer's intercession context); *Ezekiel oracle* (Eze 18:24 — prophetic case-law requiring reading within the individual accountability framework); *NT epistle* (Rom 6:15; Heb 10:26 — requiring reading within the theological argument's logical progression).

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Wisdom observation* (Ecc 7:20/26; 9:2/18; Pro 11:31/13:22): the sinner as a person-type observed from wisdom's reflective position — requires reading as general moral-anthropological observation, not specific historical accusation. (2) *Prophetic accusation* (Hos 8:11; 10:9; 2Ki 17:7): habitual defection addressed as a specific historical-covenantal failure requiring prophetic interpretation — reading requires attention to the covenant framework and historical context. (3) *Liturgical prayer* (1Ki 8:46): habitual defection addressed within a temple dedication prayer — requires reading as the prayer's realistic acknowledgment of human sinfulness within a petition for divine mercy. (4) *NT epistle/doctrinal statement* (1Jo 3:6; Heb 3:17): habitual defection addressed within theological argument — requires reading within the argument's logical structure and the new-covenant framework.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Three literary forms. (1) *Prophetic oracle/divine speech* (1Ki 16:2; 1Ki 21:22): God directly addresses the leader through a prophet — requires reading as divine judicial speech, the most authoritative form of moral assessment in the OT. (2) *Regnal narrative evaluation* (1Ki 14:16/15:26/15:30/15:34/16:13/16:19/16:26/22:52; 2Ki 17:21/21:11/21:16/21:17/23:15): the narrator's theological assessment of each king's reign — requires reading as a sustained narrative pattern with cumulative theological significance. (3) *Torah law/command* (Exo 23:33; Deu 20:18): preventive commands — requires reading within the legal-covenantal framework that establishes the expected boundaries against contagious sin.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The dominant literary form is **regnal narrative** (the narrated evaluation and record of kings' reigns in Kings and Chronicles). Responsible interpretation requires: (1) reading the pa.sha revolts as *historically narrated political events* with theological significance — the narrator presents these as facts with moral-theological weight; (2) reading the "to this day" markers as *temporal indicators from the narrator's perspective* — they name the ongoing state as the narrator experiences it; (3) reading 2Ch 21:10's causal clause ("because he had forsaken the Lord") as the *narrator's theological interpretation* of the political event — not a quote from the revolting parties but the narrator's explanatory framing.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Four literary forms across the VCGs. (1) *Dominical teaching/pronouncement* (Mat 15:19; Mat 12:31; Mar 2:7; Mar 3:28): Jesus's direct teaching — requires reading as authoritative theological pronouncement carrying the weight of the speaker's identity. (2) *Narrative-judicial* (Mat 26:65; Mar 14:64; Luk 5:21; Joh 10:33; Act 6:11): the trial and accusation scenes — requires reading within the legal-dramatic context of Jesus's trial; the blasphemy-charge is ironically inverted by the narrator's presentation of who Jesus is. (3) *Epistle-paraenesis* (Eph 4:31; Col 3:8; 1Ti 1:13; 1Ti 6:4; 2Ti 3:2): community instruction — requires reading as practical moral formation guidance for believers. (4) *Apocalyptic* (Rev 2:9; 13:1/5/6; 17:3; 2Pe 2:11; Jude 9): symbolic-visionary — requires reading within the eschatological-symbolic register; the beast's blasphemy is not a literal individual but a symbol of cosmic anti-God power.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Three literary forms across the 5 verses. (1) *Wisdom epistle* (Jam 1:14): general moral instruction on the mechanism of temptation — requires reading as practical wisdom applicable universally. (2) *Polemical epistle* (2Pe 2:14/18): warning against specific false teachers — requires reading within the polemical context; the false teachers are being characterised negatively for the readers' protection. (3) *Narrative-historical* (Act 21:21): a specific accusation in the Acts narrative — requires reading within the historical context of Paul's visit to Jerusalem and the community's concern about his teaching. (4) *Polemical epistle-narrative* (Gal 2:13): Paul recounting the Antioch incident — requires reading within Paul's argumentative context (defending the gospel against compromise).

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Four primary literary forms across the 112 verses. (1) *Theological epistle/diatribe* (Romans 3-8 — VCG-03 core): Paul's systematic theological argument addressing sin as a universal structural condition; requires reading within the logical-argumentative structure of Romans — each verse is part of a cumulative argument. (2) *Gospel narrative* (VCG-02 — Synoptic forgiveness-declarations, John's sin-conviction passages): requires reading within the narrative of Jesus's ministry; the forgiveness-declarations are situated within specific relational contexts (the paralytic's faith, the Pharisees' objections). (3) *Prophetic accusation/oracle* (VCG-01 — Isa 9:18; Mal 1:4/3:15/4:1): requires reading within the OT prophetic context of God's moral assessment of nations and communities. (4) *Acts historical narrative and epistolary* (Acts forgiveness passages; Heb 1-7; Timothy epistles): diverse literary forms requiring context-specific reading.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Three literary forms across the 9 verses. (1) *Gospel discourse* (Joh 8:34): Jesus's direct speech to opponents in the Temple — requires reading within the confrontational dialogue of John 8; the truth-claim is absolute ("truly, truly") and addressed to those who claim freedom while Jesus declares their slavery; (2) *Epistolary theology* (Rom 6:1/2; Gal 3:22): Paul's diatribe-style argument — requires reading within the logical-argumentative structure (Romans 6's liberation argument; Galatians 3's faith-versus-law argument); (3) *Johannine epistle* (1Jo 3:8/9; 5:16/18): community-instructional letters — requires reading within the pastoral-doctrinal context of distinguishing true believers from those whose practice reveals their allegiance.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Five literary forms across the 205 verses. (1) *Legal-liturgical code* (Lev 4-5; 16; Num 5; Deu 19-24): requires reading as prescriptive regulation governing the record's ritual handling; the priestly system is the institutional framework; (2) *Covenant treaty and sanction* (Lev 26; Deu 9): requires reading within the covenant-obligation context; the sevenfold discipline is calibrated covenant-sanction; (3) *Historical narrative* (Kings/Chronicles VCG-04): requires reading within the Deuteronomistic history's evaluative framework; the Jeroboam-sin formula is the historian's moral verdict; (4) *Wisdom poetry/Psalms* (Psa 25; 32; 38; 51; 59; 79; 85; 103; 109): requires reading as individual and communal prayer addressed to God; (5) *Prophetic oracle* (Isaiah; Jeremiah; Hosea; Amos; Micah; Ezekiel): requires reading within the covenantal-lawsuit or restoration-oracle framework.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Legal-priestly code* (Lev 4; 5; 10; 16; 19; 23; 25; Num 5; 29; Exo 29; 30): prescriptive regulation of the atonement system; requires reading as divine instruction with institutional force; (2) *Narrative-dialogue* (Exo 32:30/31; 2Ch 29:23/24): historical narrative of specific seeking-events; requires reading within the dramatic-covenantal context; (3) *Dominical institution narrative* (Mat 26:28): the Last Supper as Jesus's instituting act; requires reading as a constitutive speech-act that establishes the new covenant's forgiveness-ground; (4) *Apostolic proclamation and epistle* (Act 2:38; 10:43; 2Cor 5:19; Eph 1:7; Col 1:14/2:13): proclamation of received forgiveness; requires reading within the gospel-mission and community-instruction context.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Prophetic oracle/lawsuit* (Isa 43:27; Isa 64:5; Isa 65:20; Jer 8:14; Eze 37:23): requires reading within the covenantal-accusation and restoration framework; the prophet speaks as God's prosecuting attorney naming the ancestral record; (2) *Didactic historical psalm* (Psa 78:17/32): requires reading as a retelling of Israel's wilderness history designed to prevent the current generation from repeating the pattern; (3) *Intercessory prayer* (1Ki 8:47): requires reading within Solomon's temple-dedication prayer; the conditional "if" structures name the anticipated pattern of exile and confession.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Five literary forms. (1) *Narrative* (Gen 13:13; 1Sa 15:18; 1Ki 1:21; Num 16:38/32:14): requires reading within the narrative-theological framework; the sinner-identity is applied to specific historical persons and communities; (2) *Wisdom poetry* (Psa 1:1/5; Pro 1:10/13:21/23:17): requires reading within the wisdom framework of two ways; the sinner is the foil for the righteous; (3) *Prophetic oracle* (Isa 1:28/13:9/33:14; Amo 9:8/10): requires reading within covenantal-lawsuit and judgment-oracle contexts; (4) *Gospel narrative-dialogue* (Mat 9:10/11/13; Luk 5:8/7:37/18:13): requires reading within the Synoptic social-encounter framework; (5) *Epistle* (Rom 3:7/5:8/19; Gal 2:15/17; 1Ti 1:9/15; Heb 7:26/12:3; Jam 4:8/5:20; 1Pe 4:18): requires reading within the theological-instructional framework.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Legal-priestly code* (Lev 4/5/6; Num 5; Exo 28): the guilt-recognition-and-remedy sequences are prescriptive law; requires reading as normative instruction defining the conscience-to-remedy pipeline; (2) *Post-exilic confession-prayer* (Ezr 9:6-15): Ezra's penitential prayer as the guilt-accumulation expression; requires reading as a corporate confessional act within the restoration context; (3) *Historical narrative* (Gen 4:13; Gen 42:21; Gen 44:16; 1Sa 25:24/31; 2Sa 14:9): requires reading within the narrative-theological context; the guilt-experiences are part of character and plot development; (4) *Prophetic oracle* (Jer 2:22; Jer 33:8; Eze 21:23/24; Hos 5:15): requires reading within the covenantal-accusation or restoration-promise framework.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Five literary forms. (1) *Legal-cultic code* (Lev 5:1/17/7:18; Num 5:15/31; 18:1/23): the Levitical law frames iniquity as a cultic liability requiring prescribed address; (2) *Covenant proclamation* (Exo 20:5/34:7; Deu 5:9): the Decalogue context frames iniquity within God's self-description as jealous — the most authoritative frame; (3) *Wisdom literature* (Job 7:21/10:6/13:23/15:5/31:11/33; Pro 5:22; Psa 32/38/90): requires reading within the lament, reflection, and didactic wisdom contexts; (4) *Prophetic oracle* (Isa 1:4/53:5/6/11/59:2; Jer 14:7/31:34; Eze 14:3/4/7/18:20; Hos 13:12; Mic 7:18/19): requires reading within covenant-lawsuit, judgment-oracle, and restoration-promise frameworks; (5) *Confessional prayer* (Ezr 9:6/7/13; Neh 9:2; Psa 51:2/5): requires reading as corporate penitential confession within the post-exilic restoration context.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Five literary forms. (1) *Prophetic oracle/judgment* (Isa 1:2/48:8/58:1; Amos 1:3–2:6; Jer 2:8/29; Eze 2:3): requires reading within the covenant-lawsuit and judgment-announcement framework; (2) *Lament/penitential psalm* (Psa 51:1/3/13; Psa 32:1/5): requires reading within the personal confession-and-forgiveness register; (3) *Wisdom proverb* (Pro 10:12/19/12:13/17:9/19/18:19/28:13/29:6/22): requires reading within the practical-consequences framework; (4) *Legal-cultic code* (Lev 16:16/21; Exo 22:9/23:21): requires reading within the covenant-obligation framework; (5) *Epistle* (Rom 4:15/5:14/15; Gal 2:18/3:19; Heb 2:2/9:15; Jam 2:9/11): requires reading within the theological argument framework.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Four literary forms. (1) *Historical narrative* (1Ch 9:1/10:13; 2Ch 12:2/26:16/28:22/36:14; Jos 7:1/22:16-31): requires reading within the Chronicler's theological-historical framework where faithlessness is the summarising verdict on reigns and events; (2) *Legal-cultic code* (Lev 5:15/6:2/26:40; Num 5:12/27): requires reading within the covenant-obligation framework; the breaches are defined and remedied within the priestly system; (3) *Prophetic accusation/lament* (Jer 3:20/5:11; Eze 14:13/17:20/39:23; Hos 6:7; Isa 24:16/48:8): requires reading within the covenant-lawsuit framework; God or prophet as plaintiff accusing Israel of treachery; (4) *Wisdom proverb* (Pro 11:3/6/13:2/15/25:19; Psa 119:158): requires reading within the practical-consequences framework — treachery as a self-destructive character trait.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Five literary forms. (1) *Wisdom proverb* (Pro 2:12/14; 4:24; 6:14; 8:13; 10:31/32; 12:8; 16:28/30; 19:3; 23:33): requires reading within practical-moral instruction framework — perversity as a character-trait with practical consequences; (2) *Legal-cultic code* (Exo 23:8; Deu 16:19; Lev 18:23/20:12): requires reading within covenant-obligation framework — perversion as violation of God's established moral and created order; (3) *Penitential confession-prayer* (1Ki 8:47; Dan 9:5; 2Sa 24:17; Psa 38:6; Psa 106:6): requires reading within the comprehensive self-indictment register; (4) *Prophetic oracle* (Deu 32:20; Isa 29:16; Jer 2:21/3:21): requires reading within the covenant-accusation framework; (5) *Epistle* (Eph 4:22; 1Ti 6:5; 2Pe 1:4/2:12/19; Jude 10; Gal 6:8; Rom 8:21): requires reading within the theological-instructional framework.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Five literary forms. (1) *Covenant law* (Lev 19:15/35; Deu 25:16): direct divine prohibition; (2) *Wisdom literature* (Job corpus — 6:29/30/11:14/13:7/15:16/22:23/27:4/29:17/31:3/34:10/32/36:23; Pro 22:8; Psa 37:1/58:2/64:6): requires reading within the practical-moral and theodicy frameworks; (3) *Prophetic oracle* (Isa 59:3/61:8; Jer 2:5; Eze 3:20/18:8/24/26/28:15/18/33:13/15/18; Hos 10:13; Zep 3:5/13): requires reading within the covenant-accusation and judgment-oracle frameworks; (4) *Historical narrative/theological assessment* (2Sa 3:34/7:10; 2Ch 19:7): requires reading within the Chronicler's theological framework; (5) *NT epistle and gospel* (Mat 5:45; Luk 16:10/11/18:11; Rom 3:5; 1Cor 6:1/9; Heb 6:10; 1Pe 3:18; 2Pe 2:9): requires reading within the theological-ethical argument and gospel-proclamation frameworks.

---

### T7.2.3

**Question:** What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and conclusions?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *Eze 18:4/20 accountability argument*: premise — all souls belong to God; sub-premise — the sinning soul incurs its own consequence; conclusion — "the soul who sins shall die" and "the son shall not suffer for the iniquity of the father." The argument establishes individual accountability through the soul's ownership by God. (2) *1Ki 8:33–50 covenantal restoration argument*: premise — sinning against God produces defeat and disaster; premise — the path back involves turning, naming, praying; conclusion — God will forgive and grant compassion. The argument maps sinning as the cause of rupture and prayer as the causal path to restoration.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *Lev 4:2 / Num 15:27/28 — the case-law argument*: premise — a person sins unintentionally (the condition); sub-premise — the sin is real and creates liability regardless of intent; conclusion — a specific prescribed offering must be brought → the priest makes atonement → the person is forgiven. The argument establishes that *liability is act-based, not intent-based* within the covenant framework. (2) *Num 22:34 — the disclosure-confession argument*: premise — Balaam had been proceeding in a way that was morally wrong (against the angel's opposition); sub-premise — he did not know until disclosed; conclusion — upon knowing, he confesses and offers to reverse. The argument establishes that *revelation of the morally relevant fact obligates response*.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *The David pattern (2Sa 24:10)*: premise — I have sinned greatly; sub-premise — I have done very foolishly; request — please take away the iniquity. The argument moves from self-verdict to characterisation to plea: the confession is both the argument's premise and the ground of the request. (2) *The Daniel pattern (Dan 9:5–19)*: premise — we have sinned, done wrong, acted wickedly, rebelled (9:5); sub-premise — our leaders and ancestors share this record (9:8); sub-premise — the covenantal curse has fallen as stated (9:11); request — for your name's sake, act; forgive; hear; be attentive; do not delay (9:17–19). The argument is comprehensive: confession covers all dimensions of failure as the ground for appealing to God's covenantal character.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *The Jer 2:35 false innocence argument*: premise — you say "I am innocent / I have not sinned"; conclusion — God will bring you to judgment for this saying. The argument's logical force: the false claim does not escape judgment but specifically incurs it. The speech-act of denial is itself the judged act, not merely evidence of the underlying sin. (2) *The Ecc 8:12 prosperity-does-not-vindicate argument*: premise — the sinner does evil a hundred times and prolongs his life; sub-premise — "yet I know it will be well with those who fear God"; conclusion (implied) — the sinner's prosperity does not constitute vindication and does not correspond to actual moral standing. The argument challenges the false inference from prosperity to innocence.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *The Heb 10:26 argument*: premise — you have received the knowledge of truth (the full provision of Christ's sacrifice is known); conditional — if you go on sinning deliberately; consequence — there no longer remains a sacrifice for sins. The argument's logic: the sacrifice that addresses sin cannot be used by the person who deliberately continues in the sin the sacrifice was designed to address; the deliberate continuation is the condition that closes off the provision. (2) *The Eze 18:24 argument*: premise — the formerly righteous person turns to injustice and sin; consequence — none of his righteous deeds are remembered; he shall die. The argument's logic: the person's moral standing before God is determined by their current volitional direction, not by their past record; turning toward sin nullifies the prior righteousness.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *The 1Jo 3:6 biconditional argument*: premise 1 — no one who abides in him keeps on sinning; premise 2 — no one who keeps on sinning has seen or known him. The biconditional structure makes habitual sinning and abiding mutually exclusive, with seeing/knowing Christ as the epistemic condition that abiding provides and habitual sinning forecloses. (2) *The Hos 8:11 self-reinforcing argument*: premise — Ephraim multiplied altars for sinning; consequence — the altars became altars for sinning. The argument's logic: the institutionalisation of sinning produces structures that reproduce the sinning they were built for; the sinning pattern is self-sustaining through its own institutional output.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *The 1Ki 16:2 divine-charge argument*: premise — God elevated this person to leadership over his people; consequence — the person walked in Jeroboam's sin and made God's people to sin; judgment — provoking God to anger. The argument's logic: the elevation to leadership creates a proportional accountability; using the God-given authority to transmit sin is an offence compounded by the prior grace of elevation. (2) *The Exo 23:33 preventive-boundary argument*: premise — foreign nations in the land will cause Israel to sin against God; mechanism — "if you serve their gods, it will surely be a snare to you"; conclusion — therefore remove them. The argument establishes the preventive logic: eliminating the transmission pathway prevents the contagious sin from occurring.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *2Ch 21:10's theological-causal argument*: premise — Jehoram forsook the Lord, the God of his fathers; consequence — Edom and Libnah revolted from his rule; conclusion (implied) — the ruler's spiritual faithlessness produces political fragmentation. The argument establishes the theological-causal link: inner spiritual abandonment → outer political revolt. (2) *The "to this day" permanence argument* (1Ki 12:19; 2Ki 8:22; 2Ch 10:19; 2Ch 21:10): premise — a revolt occurred at a specific moment; conclusion — the revolt has persisted as a permanent political condition to the narrator's present. The argument establishes that political revolt produces lasting consequences, not temporary disruption.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Two key arguments. (1) *The Mat 12:31 forgivability argument*: premise — every sin and blasphemy will be forgiven; exception — the blasphemy against the Spirit will not be forgiven; conclusion — a threshold exists within sinful speech beyond which forgiveness is foreclosed. The argument establishes that sinful speech exists on a spectrum with a specific terminal point. (2) *The Mat 15:19 heart-origin argument*: premise — out of the heart come evil things; enumeration — murder, adultery... slander; conclusion — external ritual purity cannot address sinful speech because its source is the inner heart. The argument establishes the inner-origin principle that governs the entire characteristic.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Two key arguments. (1) *Jam 1:14's internal-desire argument*: premise — each person is tempted when lured and enticed by his own desire; implication — the locus of temptation is internal (the person's own desire is the hook), not external (God does not entice — Jam 1:13 context). The argument locates moral responsibility within the person: the enticement works through what is already present in the person. (2) *2Pe 2:14's trained-enticing-apparatus argument*: premise — hearts trained in greed, eyes full of adultery, insatiable for sin; consequence — they entice unsteady souls; judgment — "Accursed children!" The argument establishes a causal chain: formed inner character → active enticement of others → divine judgment.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Three key argument structures. (1) *The Romans 3:9-23 universal-indictment argument*: premise — Jews and Greeks are both under sin (3:9); evidence — scriptural demonstration of universal sinning (3:10-18, implied); conclusion — "all have sinned and fall short of the glory of God" (3:23). The argument establishes the universal condition by eliminating every exception. (2) *The Romans 5:12-21 one-man argument*: premise — sin entered through one man; consequence — death spread to all; antitype — one man's righteousness reverses what one man's sin established. The argument grounds the condition's universality in representative headship. (3) *The Romans 6 liberation argument*: premise — you were slaves of sin; present state — you have been freed from sin; implication — do not re-present members to sin; conclusion — present yourselves to God. The argument presupposes the condition's former total hold and the liberation's real but not yet fully realised nature.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *Joh 8:34's practice-slavery argument*: premise — everyone who practices sin is a slave to sin; implication (Joh 8:36 context) — only the Son can grant freedom; conclusion — practicing-sinners need the Son's liberation. The logic is: constituted slavery requires authoritative liberation from outside the slave-condition. (2) *1Jo 3:8/9's allegiance-birth argument*: premise 1 — whoever practices sinning is of the devil (sinning = devil-allegiance); premise 2 — no one born of God makes a practice of sinning (new birth = constitutionally anti-enslavement); conclusion — the practice of sinning and the born-of-God state are constitutionally incompatible. The logic is: deepest identity-allegiance determines whether sustained sinning is constitutionally possible.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Three key argument structures. (1) *Jer 17:1's inscription argument*: premise — the sin of Judah is permanently inscribed; implication — it cannot be self-erased; conclusion — divine intervention is the only remedy. (2) *Num 32:23's inescapability argument*: premise — you have sinned against the Lord; conclusion — your sin will find you out; the argument is that the record's self-revelation is inevitable regardless of concealment effort. (3) *Isa 43:25's divine-erasure argument*: premise — God blots out transgressions for his own sake; conclusion — he will not remember sins; the argument is that the record's erasure is entirely a sovereign divine act motivated by God's own purposes, not human merit.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Three key argument structures. (1) *Mat 26:28's institution argument*: this is my blood (ground) + poured out for many (mechanism) + for the forgiveness of sins (purpose) — the argument establishes the causal chain: covenant blood poured out produces forgiveness for the many who receive it. (2) *Act 2:38's sequence argument*: repent (human condition) + be baptized in Christ's name (human act) → forgiveness of sins (divine grant) + Holy Spirit received (divine consequence) — the argument establishes the seeking-sequence that produces receiving. (3) *Mar 3:28/29's scope-limit argument*: all sins and blasphemies will be forgiven (universal scope) BUT blasphemy against the Holy Spirit never has forgiveness (eternal limit) — the argument defines the characteristic's outer boundary by naming the single exception to its universal availability.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Two key argument structures. (1) *Isa 43:27's origin-chain argument*: premise — your first father sinned; further premise — your mediators transgressed against me; conclusion (implied) — the present community therefore carries the accumulated condition; the argument establishes the generational chain from founding act through institutional reinforcement to present community. (2) *1Ki 8:47's conditional-restoration argument*: premise — the exiles are in captivity because they sinned; condition — if they turn their heart and confess; conclusion (implied by the prayer) — then God will hear from heaven; the argument names the sought resolution to the generational condition through heart-turning and confession.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Three key argument structures. (1) *Rom 5:19's parallel-constitution argument*: premise — Adam's disobedience constituted the many as sinners; parallel — Christ's obedience will constitute the many as righteous; conclusion — the sinner-identity is neither permanent nor self-generated but follows the structure of representative-constitution; (2) *Luk 6:32/33/34's moral-minimum argument*: premise — sinners love/do good/lend within reciprocal relationships; conclusion — this is the moral baseline; implication — disciples must exceed it; the argument establishes the sinner-identity's limited-but-real moral capacity; (3) *1Ti 1:15/16's paradigmatic-sinner argument*: premise — Christ came to save sinners; evidence — Paul as the foremost sinner was saved; conclusion — this establishes the pattern for all future believers; the worst case becomes the proof of universal salvific reach.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Three key argument structures. (1) *Lev 5:2-5's discovery-guilt-remedy argument*: premise — violation committed (possibly unknowingly); when it "comes to knowledge" — guilt is realized; conclusion — the guilt must be confessed and remedied; the argument establishes the knowledge-triggered conscience-pipeline as the normative guilt-to-remedy sequence. (2) *Jer 2:22's indelibility argument*: premise — you attempt self-cleansing with lye and soap; conclusion — "the stain of your guilt is still before me"; the argument establishes that guilt cannot be self-erased; only divine action removes it. (3) *Job 11:6's debt-moderation argument*: premise — "God exacts of you less than your guilt deserves"; conclusion — divine mercy moderates the full punitive weight of guilt; the guilt-state carries a debt whose full payment God withholds.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Four key argument structures. (1) *Gen 15:16's threshold argument*: premise — the Amorites' iniquity is accumulating; conclusion — judgment withheld until completion; structure — accumulation triggers threshold-judgment; (2) *Eze 18:20's personal-accountability argument*: premise — the soul who sins shall die; conclusion — the son shall not suffer for the father's iniquity; structure — crime attaches exclusively to the individual committing it (Eze 18 counter-principle to Exo 20:5's generational visiting); (3) *Isa 53:5/6/11's vicarious-bearing argument*: premise — our iniquities crushed him; the Lord laid on him the iniquity of us all; conclusion — by bearing our iniquities the servant makes many righteous; structure — guilt-transfer through representative suffering; (4) *Psa 130:3/4's holiness-forgiveness tension argument*: premise — if God fully marked iniquities, no one could stand; but — God is a forgiver; conclusion — forgiveness is what makes God-relationship possible; structure — iniquity's weight and God's forgiveness character in productive tension.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Four key argument structures. (1) *Rom 4:15's conditional-definitional argument*: premise — the law brings wrath; premise — without law there is no transgression; conclusion — transgression is legally constituted; structure — the law's existence creates the category of transgression by establishing the boundary; (2) *Amos 1:3's threshold-judgment argument*: premise — Damascus has committed three and four transgressions; conclusion — I will not revoke the punishment; structure — accumulated threshold of violations triggers irrevocable judgment; (3) *Eze 18:28's turning-reverses-consequence argument*: premise — he committed transgressions; premise — he considered and turned from all of them; conclusion — he shall surely live; structure — volitional reversal dissolves the death-consequence; (4) *Rom 5:15/18's trespass-grace parallel*: premise — one man's trespass brought condemnation to all; conclusion — one act of righteousness brings justification for all; structure — the scale of the transgression's consequence is matched and exceeded by grace.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Four key argument structures. (1) *1Ch 10:13's death-verdict argument*: premise — Saul broke faith with the Lord (specific acts: not keeping the command, consulting a medium); conclusion — "Saul died for his breach of faith"; structure — faithlessness to God produces death as its juridical consequence; (2) *2Ch 28:22's distress-deepening paradox*: premise — Ahaz was in distress; expected conclusion — distress produces repentance; actual conclusion — "became yet more faithless"; structure — constitutional faithlessness inverts the expected distress-correction sequence; (3) *Mal 2:14-16's spirit-vigilance argument*: premise — God was witness of the covenant with the wife of one's youth; conclusion — "guard yourselves in your spirit, and do not be faithless"; structure — divine witness of the covenant creates the obligation of spirit-level vigilance; (4) *Lev 26:40-42's confession-covenant argument*: premise — "if they confess their iniquity and... treachery"; conclusion — "I will remember my covenant"; structure — honest confession of faithlessness triggers divine covenant-memory.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Four key argument structures. (1) *Exo 23:8's bribery-corruption argument*: premise — a bribe blinds the clear-sighted; conclusion — it subverts the cause of the righteous; structure — external corruption → inner blindness → injustice; (2) *Eph 4:22-24's old-self/new-self argument*: premise — the old self is being corrupted through deceitful desires; command — put it off; command — be renewed in the spirit of your mind; command — put on the new self in God's likeness; structure — diagnosis (corruption) → imperative (put off) → renewal → counter-formation; (3) *2Pe 2:19's mastery-slavery argument*: premise — these teachers are slaves of corruption; principle — "whatever overcomes a person, to that he is enslaved"; conclusion — they cannot offer freedom they do not have; structure — general mastery-principle applied to the specific corruption-case; (4) *Pro 6:14's heart-output argument*: premise — perverted heart; conclusion — devises evil continually; structure — inner faculty state → output character.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Four key argument structures. (1) *Luk 16:10's scaling argument*: premise — one who is dishonest in a very little is also dishonest in much; conclusion — dishonesty/injustice is a character-property, not a scale-dependent behaviour; structure — small-to-large scaling as character-consistency proof; (2) *Deu 32:4's definitional-negation argument*: premise — God is "without iniquity, just and upright"; structure — God's character is defined partly through the explicit negation of injustice; injustice is thus defined by what God is not; (3) *Pro 22:8's harvest argument*: premise — whoever sows injustice; conclusion — will reap calamity; structure — agricultural metaphor: the proportionate natural consequence of inner wrong; (4) *1Pe 3:18's substitution argument*: premise — "the righteous for the unrighteous"; purpose — "that he might bring us to God"; structure — Christ's righteousness applied to the unrighteous condition provides the basis for restored relationship.

---

### T7.2.4

**Question:** What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, communal, eschatological) — and what does that setting reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Four primary contextual settings. (1) *Covenantal* (VCG-01 majority — Deu 9:18; Jer 40:3/44:23; Hos 4:7; 13:2): sinning occurs within and against the Sinai covenant framework; the relational standard is the covenant Lord. (2) *Cultic/liturgical* (VCG-04 — Lev 4/5; Eze 43:23): sinning is defined relative to the sanctuary and its sacred obligations; the context is ritual purity and priestly mediation. (3) *Judicial* (Deu 19:15 — legal testimony; Gen 20:9 — Abimelech's complaint as a quasi-judicial protest): sinning is assessed in a legal-evaluative setting. (4) *Communal/discipleship* (VCG-02 NT — Mat 18:15/21; Luk 17:3/4; 1Cor 8:12): sinning is addressed within the community of disciples, requiring confrontation and forgiveness processes.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The primary contextual setting is **liturgical/cultic**: Lev 4–6 and Num 15 address sinning within the context of Israel's covenant worship system — the prescribed offerings, priestly atonement, and sanctuary purification all belong to the cultic-liturgical domain. The secondary setting is **narrative/covenantal**: Num 22:34 (Balaam and the angel — a narrative encounter with a divine messenger) and 1Sa 14:33/34 (Saul's corrective action in a military context with cultic implications). The Ezekiel VCG-02 corpus (Eze 43/45) is set within the **eschatological** sanctuary vision. The cultic-liturgical setting is dominant: CHAR-2 is primarily a characteristic addressed within Israel's formal covenant worship structures.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Covenantal* (dominant — Judg 10:10; 1Sa 7:6; Dan 9:5/11; Jer 3:25/14:20): confession occurs within Israel's covenant relationship with the Lord; the standard against which sin is named is the covenant law. (2) *Judicial/relational* (2Sa 19:20 — Shimei before the king; Mat 27:4 — Judas before the priests): confession directed at a human authority in a relational-legal context. (3) *Liturgical/prayer* (Neh 1:6; Dan 9:5; Psa 106:6): confession as the core of a formal prayer act before God. (4) *Personal/narrative* (2Sa 24:10; Luk 15:21; 1Ch 21:8): individual confession embedded in a personal moral crisis that the narrative resolves. The covenantal setting is dominant; the prayer-liturgical setting is the vehicle through which sustained confession operates.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Three contextual settings. (1) *Covenantal-prophetic* (Jer 2:35; 16:10): conscience suppression confronted within God's covenant with Israel; the prophetic word is the instrument of confrontation. (2) *Wisdom-observational* (Ecc 2:26; 8:12): conscience suppression as a general human condition observed from the wisdom perspective — the sinner-type as a recurring human phenomenon. (3) *Community-pastoral* (1Cor 15:34; Tit 3:11): conscience suppression addressed within the Christian community — both as a condition to be corrected (1Cor 15:34) and a character to be identified and handled (Tit 3:11). The three settings confirm that conscience suppression is not confined to one theological context.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Three contextual settings. (1) *Covenantal-political* (the Kings corpus — dominant): refusal to repent evaluated within the context of Israel's monarchic history and covenant with the Lord. The Jeroboam-sin formula is the covenantal-political standard. (2) *Corporate prayer-lament* (Neh 9:29): the characteristic confronted within a communal act of confession and prayer — the refusal is acknowledged as part of the community's history before God. (3) *NT theological argument* (Rom 6:15; Heb 10:26): refusal to repent addressed within doctrinal argument about the relationship between grace/law and moral accountability. Heb 10:26's eschatological-sacrificial context is the most theologically dense.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Wisdom-reflective* (Ecclesiastes and Proverbs passages): the sinner-type as an observed reality in wisdom's view of human moral life. (2) *Prophetic-covenantal* (Hosea; 2Ki 17:7): habitual defection as the covenantal failure of Israel addressed by the prophetic word. (3) *Liturgical-petitionary* (1Ki 8:46): the temple prayer as the context for realistic acknowledgment of habitual sinning and appeal for divine provision. (4) *NT community-doctrinal* (1Jo 3:6; Heb 3:17): habitual sinning addressed as a theological-existential question within the NT community of faith.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Three settings. (1) *Monarchic-covenantal* (Kings corpus — dominant): contagious sin addressed within the context of Israel and Judah's monarchic history under the Davidic covenant — the theological evaluation of each king's reign. (2) *Legal-preventive* (Exo 23:33; Deu 20:18): contagious sin addressed within the Mosaic law's boundary regulations — the covenantal boundary-setting before entry into Canaan. (3) *Prophetic-judicial* (Jer 32:35; 1Ki 16:2; 21:22): contagious sin addressed through direct prophetic word, naming specific leaders and specific acts.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Two contextual settings. (1) *Monarchic-covenantal* (dominant): the Kings and Chronicles corpus addresses political revolts within the context of Israel and Judah's monarchic governance under the Davidic covenant. The revolt is always against a king or kingdom within the covenant-historical framework. (2) *Political-military* (2Ki 3:7): the revolt becomes the occasion for military alliance-seeking — the political revolt is the context for covenant-level political diplomacy and military response.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Four settings. (1) *Didactic-discipleship* (Mat 15:19 — Jesus teaching the crowd and disciples about inner purity): heart-origin teaching in a purity-debate context. (2) *Judicial-christological* (VCG-02 — the blasphemy-charge scenes): sinful speech as a formal legal charge in the trial of Jesus. (3) *Community formation* (Eph 4:31; Col 3:8 — community paraenesis): sinful speech addressed within the practical moral formation of the NT community. (4) *Eschatological-cosmic* (Rev 13:1/5/6; 17:3): sinful speech as the defining feature of the eschatological anti-God power.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Three settings. (1) *Diaspora-universal wisdom* (Jam 1:14): addressed to dispersed believers in general; the mechanism-teaching is universal in scope. (2) *Community-protective warning* (2Pe 2:14/18): the false teachers are operating within or around the community addressed; the context is the community's need for protection from enticement. (3) *Covenantal-transitional* (Act 21:21; Gal 2:13): the Antioch incident and the Jerusalem visit are both set in the context of the covenantal transition (Jewish law and Gentile mission) that characterises the Acts narrative.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Covenant-universal* (Romans 3-5): the Jewish-Gentile universal condition addressed within the new-covenant argument about justification by faith; (2) *Priestly-liturgical* (Hebrews 1-7): the condition addressed within the high-priestly/sacrificial framework — each high priest offers for sins, Christ offers once-for-all; (3) *Baptism-repentance* (Synoptic baptism passages; Acts preaching): the condition addressed within the public repentance-and-forgiveness ritual context; (4) *Pastoral-community* (Timothy epistles; Heb 3:13): the condition addressed within the practical management of the condition's ongoing expression within the Christian community.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Three contextual settings. (1) *Disputational/judicial* (Joh 8:34): Jesus in dialogue with the Pharisees who claim Abraham's freedom; the setting is a truth-dispute about identity, freedom, and slavery before a religious authority context; (2) *Covenantal-doctrinal* (Gal 3:22): the Galatian argument about faith versus law in the new covenant context; the Scripture's imprisoning function is part of Paul's argument about the covenant's telos; (3) *Community-pastoral* (1Jo 3:8/9; 5:16/18): the Johannine community's need to distinguish those born of God from those practising sin; intercessory prayer for sinning brothers operates within the community-care context.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Priestly-liturgical* (Lev 4-5; 16): the Temple/Tabernacle ritual system as the institutional setting for managing the record through sacrifice and atonement; (2) *Covenantal-national* (VCG-04 — Kings/Chronicles; Lev 26): the Sinai covenant as the framework within which the record's accumulation triggers escalating covenant sanctions; (3) *Individual prayer and penitential Psalms*: personal and communal prayer as the setting for the record's acknowledgment and petition for erasure; (4) *Prophetic confrontation and consolation*: the prophet as the one who declares the record to those who cannot see it (Isa 58:1; Mic 3:8) and announces its future erasure (Jer 31:34; Isa 43:25).

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Priestly-tabernacle/temple* (VCG-01/03 — Leviticus, Numbers): the sacred space as the designated location for forgiveness-seeking; the Temple as the divinely appointed site of the seeking-mechanism; (2) *National covenant renewal* (2Ch 29:23/24; Exo 32:30): the corporate-national context of seeking on behalf of the whole people; (3) *Dominical institution* (Mat 26:28): the Passover meal as the context within which Jesus announces the new covenant's forgiveness-ground; (4) *Gospel proclamation* (Act 2:38; 10:43): the Pentecost preaching and the Gentile mission as the contexts within which forgiveness is offered through faith/repentance.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Three contextual settings. (1) *Covenant-accusation/restoration* (Isa 43:27; Eze 37:23): God's covenant-lawsuit against Israel naming the ancestral record, paired with the redemptive promise; (2) *Exilic-consequence* (1Ki 8:47; Jer 8:14; Isa 64:5): the exile as the setting in which the accumulated generational sinning has produced its most visible consequences; the confession and doom-recognition occur within this setting; (3) *Eschatological-new creation* (Isa 65:20): the new-creation context in which the sinner-person-type faces final curse even within the renewed world.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Three dominant contextual settings. (1) *Table-fellowship context* (VCG-01 — Mat 9:10/11/13; Mar 2:15/16/17; Luk 5:30/7:34/15:1/2): the meal as the social context in which the sinner-identity's social-stigma dimension becomes most visible; Jesus's table-fellowship with sinners is the defining social-moral provocation; (2) *Judgment-oracle context* (VCG-03 — Isa 13:9/33:14; Amo 9:8/10; Psa 104:35): the day of the Lord as the eschatological context in which the extreme-sinner-type faces destruction; (3) *Theological-argument context* (VCG-02 — Rom 5:8/19; Gal 2:15/17; 1Ti 1:15): the epistolary argument about justification, Adamic constitution, and Christ's saving mission.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Priestly-tabernacle/temple* (Lev 4/5/6; Num 5; Exo 28): the sanctuary as the designated space for guilt's ritual address; (2) *Exilic-confessional* (Ezr 9:6-15; 2Ch 28:10/13): the post-exilic community processing accumulated generational guilt; (3) *Personal narrative* (Gen 4:13; 42:21; 44:16; 1Sa 25:24/31): the guild-state experienced in personal and family crises; (4) *Prophetic-judgment* (Jer 2:22; Hos 5:15; Eze 21:23/24): the covenant lawsuit context in which guilt's indelibility and divine exposure are named.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Priestly-tabernacle context* (Lev 16:21/22; Num 18:1): the Day of Atonement as the annual setting for Israel's iniquity-bearing and removal; (2) *Covenant-proclamation context* (Exo 20:5 — Sinai theophany; Exo 34:7 — second tablets): the foundational moment of God's self-revelation as the one who both forgives and visits iniquity; (3) *Exile and return context* (Ezr 9:6/7; Neh 9:2; Lam 5:7; Jer 31:34): the post-exilic community processing accumulated generational iniquity and anticipating new covenant resolution; (4) *Prophetic-servant context* (Isa 53:5/6/11; Isa 40:2): the servant-poetry as the theological climax of the iniquity-bearing tradition.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Covenant-accusation / prophetic lawsuit context* (Isa 1:2; Jer 2:8; Eze 2:3; Amos 1:3–2:6): God as plaintiff, Israel/nations as defendant, transgression as the formal charge; (2) *Penitential-liturgical context* (Psa 51:1/3; Lev 16:16/21 — Day of Atonement): the sanctuary/prayer as the setting where transgression is confessed and addressed; (3) *Theological-argument context* (Rom 4:15/5:14/15; Gal 3:19; Heb 2:2/9:15): epistolary argument about law, transgression, and grace; (4) *Wisdom-instruction context* (Pro 10:12/19/12:13/28:13/29:6): practical teaching about transgression's relational and social consequences.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Chronicler's theological-historical verdict*: 1Ch 9:1/10:13; 2Ch 12:2/26:16/28:22/36:14 — the Chronicler's summary-assessments of reigns where faithlessness is the definitive inner characterisation of failure; (2) *Priestly-cultic ordeal and remedy*: Lev 5:15/6:2; Num 5:12/27 — the sanctuary as the setting for addressing faithlessness through prescribed ritual; (3) *Post-exilic communal confession*: Ezr 9:2/4/10:2/6/10 — the returned exile community processing accumulated faithlessness through corporate acknowledgment; (4) *Prophetic covenant-accusation*: Jer 3:20/5:11; Eze 14:13/17:20; Hos 6:7 — the prophet as covenant-plaintiff naming Israel's faithlessness before God.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Wisdom instruction context* (multiple Proverbs): the pupil being warned against perverse companions and speech; (2) *Covenant-accountability context* (Exo 23:8; Deu 16:19; Lev 18:23/20:12): justice and created-order violations named in covenant law; (3) *Exile-confession context* (1Ki 8:47; Dan 9:5; Neh 1:7): the exiled community processing accumulated perversity through comprehensive confession; (4) *NT ecclesial-ethical context* (Eph 4:22; 1Ti 6:5; 2Pe 2:12/19): false teachers' perversion threatening congregation, requiring counter-formation.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Four contextual settings. (1) *Covenant-law context* (Lev 19:15/35; Deu 25:16): the Sinai community receiving direct divine prohibition in specific institutional settings (court, marketplace); (2) *Wisdom-theodicy context* (Job corpus): Job's repeated denial of injustice in his own conduct while contending with friends who charge it; (3) *Exile-judgment context* (Eze 3:20; 18:8/24/26/33:13/15/18): Ezekiel's personal-accountability framework addressing the exiles' individual moral responsibility; (4) *NT eschatological-ethical context* (1Cor 6:9; 1Pe 3:18; 2Pe 2:9): the NT's framing of injustice as the condition requiring Christ's substitution and facing eschatological judgment.

---

### T7.2.5

**Question:** Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully and directly expresses its essential character?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Psa 51:4 functions as the primary anchor: "Against you, you only, have I sinned and done what is evil in your sight, so that you may be justified in your words and blameless in your judgment." This verse most fully and directly expresses the essential character of wilful sinning because: (1) it names sinning as a vertical relational act directed against God specifically; (2) it makes God the sole primary offended party even in a case involving human victims; (3) it frames sinning as the basis of God's righteous judgment — God's moral authority is established by the reality of human sinning; (4) it is voiced from the perspective of the sinning person in full moral awareness.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Lev 4:2 functions as the primary anchor: "If anyone sins unintentionally in any of the Lord's commandments about things not to be done, and does any one of them." This verse most fully and directly expresses the essential character of CHAR-2 because: (1) it names the inadvertent quality (*bishgagah*) as the defining criterion; (2) it frames the violation within God's commandments — the relational standard is present; (3) it establishes the case-law gate that the entire Lev 4–5 remedial system follows; (4) its "if anyone" scope is universal — any person in any situation who sins without knowing is addressed.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — 2Sa 24:10 functions as the primary anchor: "David's heart struck him... and David said to the Lord, 'I have sinned greatly in what I have done. But now, O Lord, please take away the iniquity of your servant, for I have done very foolishly.'" This verse most fully expresses the essential character of CHAR-3 because: (1) it names the conscience-origin of genuine confession ("heart struck him" before any external word); (2) it contains the full confessional formula ("I have sinned greatly") with supporting moral characterisation ("done very foolishly"); (3) it shows the natural confession-to-plea movement ("please take away the iniquity"); (4) it is God-directed — the most theologically complete direction for confession; (5) it is individual, first-person, self-initiated — the paradigm form of the characteristic.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Jer 2:35 functions as the primary anchor: "you say, 'I am innocent; surely his anger has turned from me.' Behold, I will bring you to judgment for saying, 'I have not sinned.'"
