**[CHAR-13]** E — The primary divergence: jurisprudence treats legal records as contingent human constructs — they can be expunged, sealed, or corrected through legal process initiated by human authorities. The verse evidence treats the divine record as permanent until God himself erases it: Jer 17:1 — iron pen, diamond point; Hos 13:12 — "kept in store." The erasure is entirely God's sovereign act (Isa 43:25 — "for my own sake"), not a human-initiated legal process. The divergence reveals that the divine record is not analogous to a human legal record: it is permanent, sovereignly kept, and can only be addressed by divine action — not self-erasure, not legal manoeuvre, not statute of limitations.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The primary divergence: relational psychology treats forgiveness as a process the offended party works through internally (primarily intra-psychological); the verse evidence treats forgiveness as a declarative act by God (Luk 7:48 — "your sins are forgiven") that produces an objective status-change in the forgiven person. The psychological framework focuses on the forgiver's internal process; the corpus focuses on the divine declaration that changes the forgiven person's relational standing. The divergence reveals that biblical forgiveness is not primarily a psychological process in the forgiver but a legal-relational declaration that changes the forgiven person's objective standing before God — independent of God's internal process (God does not need to "work through" forgiving in the psychological sense).

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The primary divergence: intergenerational trauma theory treats the transmission mechanism as psychological and epigenetic (neurological pathways shaped by ancestors' experiences) without moral-ontological dimension. The verse evidence treats generational sin as a *moral liability* — the present community carries actual guilt ("we have sinned" — 1Ki 8:47; Jer 8:14) not merely a psychological pattern. The divergence reveals that biblical generational sin is not reducible to psychological transmission: it involves genuine moral accountability of the present community for the inherited sinning-pattern, not merely the therapeutic challenge of breaking unhealthy behavioural cycles.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Social identity theory treats sinner-labelling as a social construction whose truth-value depends on social consensus. The verse evidence treats the sinner-identity as ontologically real: Joh 9:24's Pharisees wrongly label Jesus "a sinner" despite social consensus — the label can be socially applied and socially wrong. Virtue ethics treats character as self-formed through habituation without federal constitution. Rom 5:19 — "made sinners through Adam's disobedience" — diverges from this by introducing a constitution-event prior to any individual's habitual formation. The divergence reveals: biblical sinner-identity is not merely socially constructed (it can be wrongly applied) and not merely self-formed (it is constituted federally through Adam prior to individual habitual acts).

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Clinical psychology treats guilt as primarily an intra-psychic phenomenon that can be "excessive" or "neurotic" when it persists beyond its adaptive function. The verse evidence treats guilt as constitutively relational and objective: Lev 5:19 — "he has indeed incurred guilt before the Lord" — the guilt is real and objective regardless of subjective excess or deficit. The divergence reveals that biblical guilt cannot be pathologised as "excessive" without distorting its nature: if the guilt corresponds to actual moral liability before God, it is appropriate regardless of its felt intensity. Clinical frameworks miss the objective relational dimension (before God) that makes the guilt's intensity proportional rather than neurotic.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Bandura's framework treats moral disengagement as a cognitive error correctable through moral re-engagement. The verse evidence treats the iniquity as objective (Hos 13:12 — "bound up" regardless of disengagement) and ultimately requiring divine action for removal (Isa 53:6 — laid on the servant; Jer 31:34 — God forgives). The divergence reveals: biblical iniquity cannot be self-corrected through cognitive moral re-engagement; the crime exists in God's record independently of the person's moral-engagement state. Douglas's framework treats pollution as culturally constructed. The verse evidence treats iniquity as before God's own gaze (Jer 16:17 — "not concealed from my eyes") independently of cultural construction. Both frameworks miss the vertical-relational dimension (before God) that makes iniquity's removal require divine rather than merely human action.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Bandura's framework treats moral disengagement as a correctable cognitive error. The verse evidence treats the transgression as an objective moral reality before God (Amos 5:12 — "I know how many are your transgressions") independently of whether the transgressor engages or disengages morally. Hart's legal framework treats the law as a human social construct. Rom 4:15 treats the law as divinely promulgated, with transgression assessed against the divine standard rather than the social consensus. Ricoeur's framework treats sin primarily as lived human experience. Psa 36:1's "transgression speaks deep in the heart" adds an inner-agency dimension to transgression — as a pseudo-conscience that Ricoeur's phenomenological account does not quite capture.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Attachment theory treats disorganised attachment as produced by early relational experience rather than chosen. The verse evidence treats faithlessness as a wilful choice (1Ch 10:13 — Saul's specific acts; Lev 26:40 — "treachery they committed against me") within a constitutionally bent will (Isa 48:8 — foreknown before birth). The divergence reveals: the biblical faithlessness has both a constitutional dimension (Isa 48:8 — attachment theory may illuminate this) and a volitional dimension (1Ch 10:13 — attachment theory underweights this). The constitutional and volitional dimensions co-exist; neither framework alone captures both.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Zimbardo treats situational corruption as the primary driver (good people in bad situations do evil). The verse evidence treats the inner disposition (perverted heart — Pro 6:14) as primary, with situations as secondary triggers (Exo 23:8 — bribery triggers the perversion of what was already an assessable-corruptible judgment faculty). Aristotle treats character-formation as the product of freely chosen habituation. The verse evidence adds: Eph 4:22 — "is being corrupted through deceitful desires" — the desires themselves are named as *deceitful* (they appear good while corrupting); this is a mechanism Aristotle's voluntary-habituation framework underweights. The divergence reveals: biblical perversion operates through deceptive desire that appears good while producing corruption — a mechanism neither framework fully captures.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Tyler's procedural justice treats institutional fairness as primarily a social-psychological concern (people's sense of being respected in procedures). The verse evidence treats judicial injustice as a violation of divine standard (Deu 32:4 — God "without iniquity") before it is a violation of social expectations. Thompson's institutional corruption treats systemic injustice as primarily a structural-political problem. The verse evidence treats it as a moral-inner problem: Psa 58:2 — "in your hearts you devise wrongs" — the systemic civic injustice (Mic 3:10) begins in individual hearts before it is embedded in structures. Both frameworks underweight the inner-constitutional origin (heart) that the biblical corpus consistently names as primary.

---

### T7.3.4

**Question:** Does the human science framework surface any aspect of this characteristic that the verse evidence has not yet addressed — and does that absence require further verse investigation?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Moral psychology surfaces the question of *moral licensing* — the phenomenon where prior good behavior licenses subsequent sinning ("I've been good enough, so this one time is acceptable"). This is not explicitly evidenced in the CHAR-1 corpus as a named mechanism, though Saul's partial obedience (1Sa 15:24) has a structural resemblance: he did obey the command partially and may have regarded that partial obedience as license for the deviation. The framework also surfaces *diffusion of responsibility* (relevant to Gen 20:9 — Abraham's deception spreading sinning to Abimelech's household without their intent). These framings suggest additional analytical angles on VCG-02 interpersonal sinning that the verse evidence itself does not name but which the human science framework makes visible. No further verse investigation is required — the corpus does not need expansion, but these framings enrich the analytical description.

---

  2. Scope marker:       20× [CHAR-1] ✓
  3. Citation discipline: All E findings cite specific verses or VCGs ✓
  4. No cross-tier refs:  ✓
  5. Distinct openings:   ✓
  6. Verse-corpus check:  ✓ (all cited: Eze 18:4/20; Eze 14:13; Psa 51:4/39:1/119:11/41:4; Gen 20:6/9/39:9; Jos 7:20; 1Sa 15:24/24:11; 2Sa 12:13; 1Ki 8:33/35/50; Hos 4:7/13:2; Isa 1:4; Jer 40:3/44:23; Lev 4:14/22/23/27/28/35/5:10/13; Job 1:22/2:10; Judg 11:27; Deu 19:15; Mat 18:15/21; Luk 17:3/4; 1Cor 6:18/8:12; Gal 6:1; 1Jo 2:1; 2Pe 2:4; Pro 8:36/19:2; Ecc 5:6; Neh 6:13/13:26 — all in §3)
  → PASS. All 8 tiers complete.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The bounded-rationality framework surfaces the question of *preventability*: moral philosophy asks whether the unintentional actor could have known (i.e., whether the information gap was itself culpable through negligence). The verse evidence does not address the question of whether the person *should have known* — Lev 4:2's case-law provision makes no distinction between unavoidable ignorance and negligent ignorance. This gap in the verse evidence suggests that CHAR-2's legal-moral logic does not grade inadvertent sinning by the degree of preventability — any inadvertent violation, however hard it would have been to prevent, receives the same remedial provision. This uniformity is itself a theological statement: the provision is comprehensive rather than means-tested. No further verse investigation is required — the corpus does not address the preventability question and this silence appears deliberate.

  → PASS. All 8 tiers complete.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The social-psychology research on self-disclosure surfaces the question of *audience effect*: confession before a community (VCG-02 — Judg 10:10; 1Sa 7:6; Psa 106:6) differs psychologically from private confession to God (VCG-01 — 2Sa 24:10) — public confession creates social accountability that private confession does not. The verse corpus treats both as equally valid confession forms without differentiating their social-psychological dynamics. The framework also surfaces the question of *repeat confession* — whether confessing the same sin repeatedly (Neh 1:6 — "day and night") has different psychological dynamics than single-episode confession. The verse evidence does not address the psychology of repetition, which may be worth noting as a gap in the corpus's treatment of sustained confession. No further verse investigation is required — the corpus does not raise these questions; the framework surfaces them for potential future analytical attention.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The social psychology research on *pluralistic ignorance* — where individuals within a community suppress their own private concerns because they assume others are not concerned — illuminates Jer 16:10's communal dimension: the people collectively ask "what sin?" partly because the entire community has suppressed moral awareness together, each reinforcing the others' non-recognition. The individual's conscience suppression is sustained by the community's shared moral blindness. The verse evidence implies but does not name this communal reinforcement mechanism; the framework makes it analytically visible. No further verse investigation is required — the corpus does not address this mechanism explicitly, but the framework enriches the communal reading of Jer 16:10.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Addiction research surfaces the question of the *withdrawal cost* of departure — the phenomenon where the person cannot easily stop the sinful behaviour because departure involves real experiential cost (the removal of psychological reinforcement). This is not named in the verse evidence (which assumes departure is always available and the refusal is volitional) but illuminates why the *davaq* (clinging) mode of CHAR-5 is more resistant to correction than the passive non-departure mode: the person who clings has a stronger psychological bond to the sinful course than the person who merely has not departed. The framework does not require further verse investigation — the corpus does not address the subjective phenomenology of departure-cost; the framework enriches the reading of 2Ki 3:3 without requiring new data.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Institutional sociology surfaces the *maintenance cost* of habit-breaking: breaking an institutionally embedded sinning pattern (Hos 8:11's altars) requires not only an inner-disposition change but the dismantling of the social structures that reproduce the pattern. The verse evidence does not address the institutional dismantling process (the altars of Hos 8:11 would need to be physically removed as well as the inner orientation changed). This gap in the verse evidence is analytically real: the corpus describes how habitual defection institutionalises but not how the institutional deposit is reversed. 1Jo 3:6's abiding in Christ addresses the inner dispositional change but does not specify the dismantling of the social-structural deposit. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as a limitation of the corpus.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Leadership influence research surfaces the question of *follower moral agency*: research distinguishes between followers who receive and replicate the leader's moral orientation and followers who maintain independent moral assessment. The verse evidence does not address the inner moral agency of individual community members who receive the contagious sin transmission (T4.4.3 — noted as silent). The framework raises the question of whether and how individual community members can resist the contagious sin transmission — a question the corpus does not answer for the individual community member, though Josiah's leadership reversal (2Ki 23:15) models the counter-formation capacity at the leadership level. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is structural to the corpus's focus on the leader's accountability rather than the individual community member's response.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Weber's authority framework surfaces the question of *authority-succession dynamics*: the verse evidence (Moab revolting specifically after Ahab's death) implies that succession transitions are the primary vulnerability moment for governing authority — a pattern Weber's framework explains through the "routinisation of charisma" problem. The verse evidence does not address why succession transitions are the vulnerable moment; the framework supplies the structural explanation. No further verse investigation is required — the corpus does not address succession dynamics explicitly; the framework enriches the reading of 2Ki 1:1 / 3:5 without requiring new verse data.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Verbal aggression research surfaces the *recipient's experience*: the psychological literature documents the lasting harm of verbal aggression on recipients (depression, diminished self-regard, relational damage). The verse evidence addresses Rev 2:9's recipients (Smyrna community) and Act 6:11's Stephen as targets, but does not describe the inner-being experience of receiving sinful speech in detail. The framework raises the question of what sustained reception of sinful speech produces in the inner being of the recipient — a question the corpus addresses at the theological level (Rev 2:9 — "I know your tribulation... but you are rich") but not at the psychological level. No further verse investigation is required; the gap reflects the corpus's theological rather than psychological focus.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Addiction science surfaces the *neural pathway reconsolidation* process — the mechanism by which craving-pathways are rewired through sustained engagement with alternative objects. The verse evidence (specifically the positive implied by 2Pe 2:14's "insatiable for sin" — the implication that satiation with God-directed desire would be the corrective) does not address the specific mechanism of desire-reorientation. The gap: how does the heart trained in greed become a heart trained in righteousness? The verse evidence implies the possibility of reversal (Jam 1:14's enticement is resisted; Gal 2:13's hypocrisy is confronted by Paul) but does not detail the reorientation mechanism. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as outside this characteristic's scope.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Depth psychology surfaces the *phenomenological texture* of living under the condition: what it feels like from within to be caught between the good one intends and the evil one does. The Romans 7 evidence addresses this structure (the divided-person experience) but not its experiential texture. Psychology of shame (distinct from guilt) — the feeling of being fundamentally defective rather than merely having done wrong — would be relevant to Joh 9:34 ("born in utter sin") and Eph 2:1 ("dead in trespasses and sins"): these verses describe an ontological condition (being born in sin; being dead) that corresponds to shame-experience rather than mere guilt-experience. The corpus addresses the guilt-dimension (debt requiring forgiveness — VCG-02) but less directly the shame-dimension (being-condition rather than doing-act). No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as a psychological complement to the corpus's primarily theological framing.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Addiction science surfaces the *environmental and social reinforcement* dimension of compulsive behaviour patterns: the enslaving power is not merely internal (neural pathways) but reinforced by relational and environmental contexts that enable and sustain the practice. The 9-verse corpus addresses the individual practice (Joh 8:34), the community context (1Jo 5:16 — the sinning brother visible to others), and the adversarial spiritual reinforcement (1Jo 3:8 — the devil's domain) but does not explicitly address the social/environmental contexts that sustain sinning practice. The framework suggests that the community's intercessory role (1Jo 5:16 — asking for the sinning brother) may address this environmental dimension: the community's prayer is the social-relational counter-reinforcement against the enslaving practice. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement to the corpus's primarily theological framework.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Moral psychology surfaces the *intergenerational transmission* of guilt through communal-narrative processes — the way in which communities pass on moral records through story, ritual, and identity formation. VCG-04's Jeroboam-sin legacy is precisely this: a communally transmitted moral record that successive generations inherit through the narrative of their community's founding sin-pattern. The framework suggests that the mechanism of VCG-04's transmission is not merely legal or covenantal but also narrative and identity-forming — the Jeroboam-sin legacy functions as a shared moral identity narrative that each king either receives and perpetuates or breaks from. This narrative-identity dimension of the record's generational transmission is acknowledged but not fully theorised in the corpus. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement to the corpus's primarily covenantal framing.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Relational psychology surfaces the *experience of the forgiven person integrating the forgiveness* — what it is like to live as a forgiven person over time, the process of accepting and internalising the objective status-change. The verse evidence addresses the transformation consequences (love — Luk 7:47; life — Col 2:13; Spirit — Act 2:38) but does not describe the process of the forgiven person integrating the forgiveness into their ongoing self-understanding. The gap: the subjective phenomenology of living as a forgiven person (distinct from the objective status-change). The gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement; no further verse investigation is required within this characteristic's scope.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Intergenerational trauma theory surfaces the *protective factors* that enable individuals within affected communities to break the generational pattern without external catastrophe (without needing exile and doom to produce recognition). Isa 64:5 — "those who remember you in your ways" — names the positive inner orientation but does not explain the psychological or formative processes that produce it in some individuals while others perpetuate the pattern. The framework suggests that deliberate counter-practices (ritual, collective memory, community formation) can enable pattern-breaking — a dimension the corpus addresses only through divine intervention (Eze 37:23) and external catastrophe (exile — 1Ki 8:47). No further verse investigation is required — the gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Social identity theory surfaces the dynamics of *stigma management* — how persons bearing the sinner-label negotiate their identity in social contexts. Luk 7:37/39's woman of the city navigates the social-stigma dimension by approaching Jesus in a context where her sinner-label creates social risk; Luk 18:13's tax collector manages the stigma through radical self-abasement that pre-empts social judgment. The verse evidence describes these behaviours but does not theorise the identity-management strategies involved. The gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement; no further verse investigation is required — the sinner-identity's social dynamics are present in the corpus but the management-strategy dimension is underspecified.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Clinical psychology surfaces the *reparative function* of guilt more precisely than the corpus does: Tangney's research shows that productive guilt consistently motivates reparative behaviour toward the wronged party. The verse evidence confirms this (Lev 6:4 — guilt → restitution; Num 5:7 — guilt → confession + full restitution + added fifth) but does not theorise the psychological mechanism by which guilt produces the motivation. The gap is the psychological mechanism of guilt-to-reparation motivation: the corpus describes the sequence but leaves the inner motivational mechanism implicit. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is a human-science complement to the corpus's prescriptive framework.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Bandura's framework surfaces the dynamics of *collective moral disengagement* — how groups rationalise shared iniquity — that parallels the communal self-deception evidenced in Jer 16:10 ("what is our iniquity?") and Hos 12:8. The gap is how communities maintain collective iniquity-denial despite accumulating evidence of divine displeasure: the social-psychological dynamics of communal conscience suppression are present in the corpus (Jer 16:10; Hos 12:8) but underspecified at the group-psychology level. No further verse investigation is required — the gap is a human-science complement; the corpus describes the communal denial-pattern without theorising its social mechanism.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Hart's legal framework surfaces the concept of *secondary rules* (rules about rules) that CHAR-19 might engage: the question of when the threshold of transgression has been reached (Amos 1:3 — "three and four") is a secondary-rule question about how primary rule-violations are counted and adjudicated. The gap is the decision-procedure by which God adjudicates the threshold — the mechanism of "I will not revoke the punishment" after the three-and-four count. The verse evidence names the outcome (judgment after threshold) without specifying the decision-procedure. No further verse investigation is needed; the gap is a legal-philosophical complement to the corpus's theological declaration.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Attachment theory surfaces the dynamics of *re-attachment after betrayal* — how the betrayed party (God in VCG-01) can sustain the relationship through the betrayal and remain available for restoration. The verse evidence describes God hiding his face (Eze 39:23) and then restoring (Lev 26:42 — "I will remember my covenant") — but does not theorise the inner divine process by which the covenant-faithful God maintains availability through the betrayal. The gap is the mechanism of divine covenant-faithfulness sustaining the relationship through the experience of covenant-faithlessness. No further verse investigation is needed; the gap is a theological-anthropological complement.

---

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Zimbardo's framework surfaces the *normalisation of corruption* — how gradual situational corruption makes perversity appear normal or even virtuous. The verse evidence includes this in the Pro 2:14 "delight in the perverseness of evil" — the affective normalisation of perversity — and Isa 29:16's creator-denial as the cognitive normalisation of the creature's inversion. But the social-psychological mechanism by which gradual exposure to corruption normalises it is underspecified in the corpus. The gap is acknowledged as a human-science complement; no further verse investigation is needed.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Thompson's institutional corruption surfaces the *systemic normalisation of injustice* — how institutional structures can make individual wrongdoing appear legitimate or even required. Psa 82:2's judges have been doing this: systematically showing partiality to the wicked. The gap is the social-psychological mechanism by which individual judicial injustice (Psa 82:2 — showing partiality) is normalised across the judicial institution. No further verse investigation is needed; the gap is a human-science complement to the corpus's internal anatomy of injustice's social consequences.
